Normal Probability Distribution
The normal/Gaussian probability distribution is the most common
probability continuous distribution.
It’s used in a wide variety of practical applications.
It is widely applied to statistical inference
Something we will be using all the time from now on
The normal distribution assumes the classic bell-shaped curve and
provides a description of the likely results obtained through sampling.
We will talk about sampling next week.
Derived and proved by Abraham de Moivre, a French mathematician.
Work published on The Doctrine of Chances in 1733.
Same year the Molasses Act was imposed
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/19/2024 2 / 32
Normal Probability Density Function
The normal probability density function is described by two parameters:
the meanµand the standard deviationσ.
Don’t write it down. Too much work! ;)

Figure 1: Normal DistributionIan Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/19/2024 3 / 32
The Mean of the Normal Distribution
We make the following observations about the meanµof the normal
distribution:
1 The meanµis the highest point on the normal curve.
2 The meanµis also the median and mode of the distribution.
3 The mean of the distribution can be any numerical value: negative, zero,
or positive.
4 The normal distribution or the bell-shaped curve is symmetric.
5 The area under the curve to the left of the mean and the area under the
curve to the right of the mean are both 0.50.
6 The tails of the normal curve extend to infinity in both directions and
theoretically never touch the horizontal axis.

The Spread of the Normal Distribution
We make the following observations about the standard deviationσof
the normal distribution:
1 The standard deviation determines how flat and wide the normal curve is.
2 Larger values ofσresult in wider, flatter curves, showing more variability
in the data.
The figure shows two normal distributions with the same mean butσ=
5 andσ=10.
3 The mean determines the location of the curve, SD determines the
shape of the curve.

Standard Normal Probability Distribution
We need a tool to help us unify or convert these non-standard normal
distributions to a standard normal distribution.
A random variable having a normal distribution witha mean of 0 and
a standard deviation of 1 is said to have a standard normal
probability distribution.
The standard normal distribution has the same general appearance as
other normal distributions, but with the special properties of having
µ= 0 andσ= 1
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/19/2024 10 / 32
Standard Normal Probability Distribution
Because ofµ= 0 andσ= 1, the formula for the standard normal
probability density function is a simpler version of the equation shown
earlier for the general normal probability distribution:
f (x ) = 1√
2πe− x 2
2 (2)

We use the following formula to convert any normal random variablex
with meanµand standard deviationσto the standard normal random
variable z
z = x−µ
σ

Standard Normal Probability Distribution
As with other continuous random variables, probability calculations
with a normal distribution are made by computing the area under the
graph of the normal curve over a given interval.
We will now show how to use the Z-Table to calculate the following
cumulative probabilities associated with a standard normal probability
distribution
The probability that the standard normal random variablez will be less
than or equal to a given valueZ: P(z≤Z1)
The probability thatz will be between two given values,
Z1 and Z2 : P(Z1≤z≤Z2)
The probability thatz will be greater than or equal to a given value
P(z≥Z2)
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/19/2024 14 / 32
Standard Normal Probability Distribution
The probability that the standard normal random variablez will be less
than or equal to a given valueZ1: P(z≤Z1)
The probability thatz will be between two given values,
Z1 and Z2 : P(Z1≤z≤Z2)
The probability thatz will be greater than or equal to a given value
P(z≥Z2)
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/19/2024 15 / 32
Cumulative Probability for P(z≤1.00)
The cumulative probability thatz is less than or equal to 1.00, that is,
P(z≤1.00), is the area under the standard normal curve to the left of
z = 1.00
To find the cumulative probability corresponding toz≤1.00 in the
Z-Table,
We identify the value located at the intersection of the row labeled 1.0
and the column labeled 0.00.
We find 1.0 in the left column of the table
We find 0.00 in the top row of the table.

P(z≤1.00) = 0.8413

Cumulative Probability for P(−0.50≤z≤1.25)
Three steps are required to compute this probability using the Z-Table
1 We calculate P(z≤−0.50) as the intersection of the –0.5 row and the
0.00 column:
P(z≤−0.50) = 0.3085
2 We calculate P(Z≤1.25) as the intersection of the 1.2 row and the
0.05 column:
P(z≤1.25) = 0.8944
3 We calculate the difference of the cumulative probabilities to the left of
z = 1.25 and z =−0.50 that we calculated in steps 1 and 2, so that:
P(−0.50≤z≤1.25) = P(z≤1.25)−P(z≤−0.50) = 0.5859
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/19/2024 19 / 32
Cumulative Probability for P(z≥1.58)
The cumulative probability thatz is greater than or equal to 1.58, that
is, P(z≥1.58), is the area under the standard normal curve to the
right of z = 1.58.
To find the probability to the right ofz = 1.58, we need to first find
the probability corresponding to the left ofz = 1.58, and then
calculate its complement.
To calculate the area to the left ofz = 1.58, identify the value located
at the intersection of the row labeled 1.5 and the column labeled 0.08.
Thus, P(z≤1.58) = 0.9429
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/19/2024 20 / 32
Cumulative Probability for P(z≥1.58)
However, because the total area under the normal curve is 1, we have
P(z≥1.58) = 1−0.9429 = 0.0571

Find Z Such That P(z≥z∗) = 0.10
We want to find az ∗ value such that the probability of obtaining a
larger z value is 0.10. That is
P(z≥z ∗) = 0.10
In this inverse problem, we need to find thez value that corresponds to
a given probability.
However, the standard normal probability table gives the area under
the curve to the left of a particularz value.
Thus, we use again the complement rule to find az ∗ value such that
P(z≤z ∗) = 1−P(z≥z ∗) = 1−0.10 = 0.90

Grear Tire Company Problem
Grear Tire Company has developed a new steel-belted radial tire to be
sold through a national chain of discount stores. From tire road tests,
Grear’s engineering group estimated
Mean tire mileage:µ= 36,500 miles
Standard Deviation:σ= 5,000 miles
In addition, the data collected indicate that a normal distribution is a
reasonable assumption.
Thus, we define a normal random variable asx = number of miles the
tire will last
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/19/2024 24 / 32
Grear Tire Company Problem
Before finalizing the tire mileage guarantee policy, Grear’s managers
want an answer to the following two questions:
What is the probability that the tire mileage will exceed 40,000 miles?
What should the guaranteed mileage be if Grear wants no more than
10% of the tires to be eligible for a discount?
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/19/2024 25 / 32
Probability Tire Mileage Exceeds 40,000 Miles
As depicted by the darker area in the figure, we need to find
P(x≥40,000).
We can convert the normal random variablex to the standard normal
random variable z, as
z = x−µ
σ = 40,000−36,500
5,000 = 3,500
5,000 = 0.70
The probability thatz is greater than or equal to 0.70 is the
complement to the probability thatz is less than or equal to 0.70.
Thus,
P(x≥40,000) = P(x≥0.70) = 1−P(z≤0.70) = 1−0.7580 = 0.2420
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/19/2024 26 / 32
Probability Tire Mileage Exceeds 40,000 Miles
We can conclude that about 24.2% of the tires will exceed 40,000 in
mileage.
Figure 14: Tire Mileage Example

What should the guaranteed mileage be if Grear
wants no more than 10% of the tires to be eligible
for a discount?
In this inverse problem, we need to find a value ofx ∗ such that:
P(x≤x ∗) = 0.10
The Z-Table shows the closest probability to 0.1000 to be 0.1003,
corresponding to the -1.2 row and the 0.08 column.
That is, z ∗ =−1.28. Thus, we have
z ∗ = x ∗−µ
σ
Solving for x ∗, we find
x ∗ =µ−1.28×σ= 36,500−1.28(5,000) = 30,100 miles

Why Interval Estimation?
You don’t want to give an overly confident answer for something you
are not very sure.
It’s like a way of giving a smart guess with a buffer zone, backed with
statistical evidence, knowing you won’t always be 100% precise, but
you’ll be close enough most of the time.
Imagine you’re launching a new product, and you’re trying to guess
how much profit it will make. An interval estimate is like saying, “We
don’t know exactly, but we estimate the profit will be between $50,000
and $80,000.”
You are going out with friends and your parents ask you when you are
gonna be home? Say something-ish, not an exact time.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/24/2024 4/40
Introduction
A point estimator is a sample statistic used to estimate a population
parameter.
The sample mean¯x is a point estimator of the population meanµ
The sample proportionˆp is a point estimator of the population
proportion p
The sampling distributions of¯x and ˆp play key roles in computing
these interval estimates.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/24/2024 5/40
Introduction
An interval estimateis computed by adding and subtracting a
value(called the margin of error) to the point estimate. Thus, the
general forms of an interval estimate of a population mean and
proportion are:
¯x ±Margin of Error and ˆp ±Margin of Error
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/24/2024 6/40
Margin of Error and the Interval Estimate
A point estimatorcannot be expected to provide the exact value of
the population parameter.
But we want to try hard to get closer and closer to the exact
value/truth.
An interval estimatecan be computed by adding and subtracting a
margin of error to the point estimate.
The purpose of an interval estimate is to provide information about
how close the point estimate is to the value of the parameter.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/24/2024 7/40
Interval Estimate of a Population Mean: When the
σ Is Known
In order to develop an interval estimate of a population mean, the
margin of error must be computed using either:
The population standard deviationσ, or
The sample standard deviations
σis rarely KNOWN exactly(why?). But often a good estimate can be
obtained based on historical data and/or other information available
We refer to such cases as theσKNOWN case
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/24/2024 8/40
Interval Estimate of a Population Mean: When the
σ Is NOT Known
Let us construct an interval estimate ofµ, the mean amount spent
per shopping trip for the population of Lloyd’s customers, in a case
for which it is reasonable to treatσas known, given:
n = 100 customers, a simple random sample
x = the amount spent per shopping trip
s =σ= 20, based on historical data
The population follows a normal distribution
Calculations reveal that the sample mean
a point estimate of population meanµ, is: ¯x = $82.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/24/2024 9/40
A New Greek Letterα
αis the significance level(or the level of significance) and is used to
calculate a confidence interval(which is often given).
The confidence level is calculated as 100∗(1−α).
It represents the probability of obtaining certain results by chance.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/24/2024 10/40

can be written as
¯x±zα/2
σ√n
Where, 1−αis the confidence coefficient, andzα/2(the z value
providing an area ofα/2 in the upper tail of the standard normal
probability distribution.

Sampling Distribution for the Sample Mean
This sampling distribution depicts how values of¯x are distributed
around the population meanµ
It follows that 95% of the values of the normally distributed¯x random
variable falls within±1.96σ¯x standard deviations of the mean, or
within
±1.96σ¯x =±1.96(2) =±3.92
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/24/2024 13/40
The Confidence Level
Let us set the margin of error equal to 3.92 and compute the interval
estimate ofµusing ¯x±3.92.
The figure in the next slide shows what could happen if we took three
separate samples of 100 Lloyd’s customers and built an interval
estimate for each of them.
The first two intervals, built around the sample means¯x1 and ¯x2,
include the population mean,µ, but the third one,¯x3, does not.
It turns out that 95% of all intervals formed by subtracting 3.92 from
¯x and adding 3.92 to¯x will include the population mean,µ
We call an interval built around¯x a confidence interval established at
the 95% confidence level.

Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/24/2024 22/40
Practical Advice(You May Get Asked by An
Interviewer)
Adequate Sample Size
The sampling distribution of¯x is normal if the underlying population
distribution is normal; otherwise, a sample size ofn≥30 is required.
If the population distribution is highly skewed or contains outliers, a
sample size of 50 or more is recommended.
If the population is not normally distributed but is roughly symmetric,
a sample size as small as 15 will suffice.
If the population is believed to be at least approximately normal, a
sample size of less than 15 can be used.
For small sample sizes, a histogram of the sample data should be built
to learn about the shape of the population distribution.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/24/2024 23/40
Why Interval Estimation?

Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/24/2024 24/40
Population Mean: σ Unknown
The t Distribution
If an estimate of the population standard deviation,σ, cannot be
developed prior to sampling, we use the sample standard deviation,s,
instead.
This is referred to as theσunknown case
With the interval estimate ofµbased on thet distribution, which is a
family of similar probability distributions depending on a parameter
called the degrees of freedom.
A t distribution with more degrees of freedom has less dispersion.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/24/2024 25/40
What is the Degree of Freedom?
Degrees of Freedom refer to the number of independent pieces of
information (data points) that go in the computation of the sample
standard deviation, s, and with thenth data point being “locked” by
the computations of the sample mean,¯x, needed for the computation
of s.
n−1 is how we determine the degree of freedom
Why n−1?
Imagine you’re at a party with a group of friends, and you’ve decided
to line up for a photo. If the photographer asks everyone to spread out
evenly across the frame, the first few friends can choose their spots
freely, but the last friend’s position is determined by everyone else’s
choice to maintain the balance.
In statistics, this is like havingn−1 degrees of freedom: forn friends,
n−1 can choose their spot freely, while the last spot is constrained by
the overall arrangement,n−1 data points are free to vary, but the last
is determined by the sum needed to meet the mean.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/24/2024 26/40
Population Mean: σ Unknown
As the degrees of freedom increase, the difference between thet
distribution and the standard normal probability distribution becomes
smaller and smaller
For more than 100 degrees of freedom, the standard normal z value
provides a good approximation to thet value
Keep this in mind: if the degrees of freedom exceed 100, the standard
normal z value provides a good approximation to the t value.
Figure 3: Standard Normal Distribution andt distribution
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/24/2024 27/40

An interval estimate of the population meanµ, for theσunknown
case, can be written as
¯x±tα/2
s√n
Where s is the sample standard deviation, 1−αis the confidence
coefficient, and tα/2 is the t value providing an area ofα/2 in the
upper tail of at distribution with n−1 degrees of freedom
Figure 4: t Distribution

the row with 69 degrees of freedom, and the column corresponding to

Interval Estimate ofµ: σ Unknown Example

the margin of error.
Thus, we are 95% confident that the mean credit card balance for the

Determining the Sample Size for the Estimate ofµ
Given a desired the margin of errorE, selected prior to sampling such
that
Error = zα/2
σ√n
The sample size necessary for thedesired margin of error can be
determined solving forn
n = (zα/2)2σ2
E 2

Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan 22 or 27, 2026 4/61
What is Data?
Data refers to raw facts, figures, and details collected from various
sources that are used for analysis, decision-making, or understanding
phenomena. Data can take many forms, including numbers, text,
images, and more. It can be structured, like in tables and databases,
or unstructured, like emails or social media posts.
In essence, data is the basic building block from which information is
derived. When processed, interpreted, or analyzed, data becomes
meaningful information that can help answer questions, solve
problems, and guide decision-making.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan 22 or 27, 2026 5/61
What is Data?
Data are thefacts and figurescollected, analyzed, and summarized
for presentation and interpretation.
Elements are the entities on which data are collected.
A variable is a characteristic of interest for the elements.
A dataset consists of all the data collected for a particular study.
The total number of data values in a complete data set is the number
of elements multiplied by the number of variables.
An observation is the set ofmeasurements obtained for a particular
element.
A data set with n elements contains n observations.

Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan 22 or 27, 2026 9/61
Scales of Measurement
What is the scale of measurement?
It determines theamount and types of information contained in the
data.
It indicates the data summarization and statistical analyses that are
most appropriate.
Scales of measurement
Nominal: the data are labels or names used to identify an attribute of
the element.
Ordinal: the data have the properties of nominal data, and the order
or rank of the data is meaningful.
Interval: the data have the properties of ordinal data, and the interval
between observations is expressed in terms of a fixed unit of measure.
Data with an interval scale of measurement are always numerical.
Ratio: the data have the properties of interval data, and the ratio of
two values is meaningful.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan 22 or 27, 2026 10/61
Scales of Measurement Examples
Nominal: In a study examining different industries, each industry can
be categorized nominally such as ‘Technology’, ‘Healthcare’,
‘Finance’, ‘Retail’, etc. Here, the categories are mutually exclusive
and have no inherent order.
Ordinal: Surveys often ask customers to rate their satisfaction on a
scale, such as ‘Very Unsatisfied’, ‘Unsatisfied’, ‘Neutral’, ‘Satisfied’,
‘Very Satisfied’. This ordinal scale indicates an order of satisfaction
but doesn’t specify the exact difference in satisfaction between each
category.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan 22 or 27, 2026 11/61
Scales of Measurement Examples
Interval: In performance appraisals, employees might be rated on a
scale of 1-10 on various attributes like teamwork, communication, and
skill level. This scale has equal intervals (the difference between 2 and
3 is the same as between 8 and 9), but there is no true zero point (a
score of 0 doesn’t imply the absence of the attribute).
Ratio: Financial figures such as annual revenue are measured on a
ratio scale. They have a meaningful zero point (zero revenue means
no sales), and the intervals between figures are consistent. A
company with $200,000 in revenue has twice as much as one with
$100,000, demonstrating the properties of a ratio scale.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan 22 or 27, 2026 12/61
Data Types
Data can be further classified ascategorical or quantitative.
This helps choose the statistical analysis that is appropriate depends on
whether the data for the variable are categorical or quantitative.
E.g. In machine learning, models and approaches to process categorical
and quantitative data are completely different.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan 22 or 27, 2026 13/61
Categorical Data - Variables w/ Categorical Data.
What is it?
Nominal data represents categories that do not have a natural order or
ranking.
Ordinal data consists of categories that have a natural order or ranking
but the intervals between the ranks may not be equal.
They are used for labeling or naming attributes of elements in a
dataset.
Why do we use it?
Simplification of Complex Information.
Useful in Identifying Trends and Patterns.
Facilitation of Group Comparisons.
What are some of the examples?
Types of payment methods, industry sectors, nationalities, customer
satisfaction levels, education levels, sizes of clothing.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan 22 or 27, 2026 14/61
Quantitative Data - Variables w/ Quantitative Data
What is it?
Quantitative data is the numerical data that can be measured and
quantified.
Types of quantitative data
Discrete dataare the countable items or values that are distinct and
separate. The values typically represent counts of objects or
occurrences.
Cannot subdivided into finer and finer increments.
Limited possibilities(e.g. 3 customers, 6 flights, 125 students)
Continuous: data are the measurements and can take on any value
within a range.
Can subdivided into finer and finer increments.
Unlimited possibilities(e.g. MPG at 17.7666753333, Housing Price at
$350,888.8383345)
What are some of the examples?
Number of employees in a company, number of products sold in a day,
stock prices over time, sales revenue over a year, measured in dollars.

Data

1 Mean: The sum of all the values in a data set divided by the number

Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan 22 or 27, 2026 29/61
What More Can We Do By Using The Mean, or
Other Measures of The Central Tendency?
Now we have learned how the data“looks”like, and we want to
make the BEST use of it!
Statistical inference is a process used to make conclusions or
predictions about a larger dataset based on a smaller dataset, by
using key characteristics such as the measures of the central tendency.
Measures of the Central Tendency are just the characteristics of the
data.
Knowing the basic characteristics helps us make predictions about the
larger, similar datasets.

Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan 22 or 27, 2026 31/61
Statistical Inference
Statistical inference can help you predict the future events:
Statistical inference is a fundamental part of statistics.
Involves drawing conclusions about a large dataset based on data
randomly selected(sampled) from it.
In simpler terms, it’s about making educated guesses about a larger
group using information from a smaller group taken from that larger
one.
The process of statistical inference typically involves estimating
population parameters and testing hypotheses.
That’s something we will learn together later in the semester.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan 22 or 27, 2026 32/61
Statistical Inference
Population: The set of all elements of interest in a particular study.
Example: All the customers who have ever purchased products from
Amazon.com
Sample: A subset of the population.
Example: A group of 1,000 customers randomly selected from
Amazon’s database
Statistical inference: The process of using data obtained from a
sample to make estimates and test hypotheses about the
characteristics of a population.
Example: Finding statistical evidence that the average spending per
visit for all customers on Amazon is $50, based on the analysis of the
sample data.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan 22 or 27, 2026 33/61
Statistical Inference
Census: Collecting data for the entire population.
Collecting data on every individual resident’s age, occupation, and
marital status in a country during the national census.
Sample survey: Collecting data for a sample.
Example: Conducting an online survey where a group of 1000 college
students are asked about their favorite foods and drinks.

Definition of Statitical Inference
Statistical inference is a process used to make conclusions or
predictions about a population based on a smaller sample.
Measures of the Central Tendency are essential to make inferences.
Knowing the basic characteristics of dataset helps us make
predictions/guesses about the entire population.

Analytics
Analytics is the scientific process of transforming data into insightful
and intuitive conclusions for making better decisions.
Descriptive analytics: This describes what has happened in the past.
Predictive analytics: Use models constructed from past data to predict
the future or to assess the impact of one variable on another.
Prescriptive analytics: The set of analytical techniques that yield a best
course of action.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan 22 or 27, 2026 39/61
Ethical Guidelines for Statistical Practice
In a statistical study, unethical behavior can take a variety of forms
including:
Improper sampling
Inappropriate analysis of the data
Development of misleading graphs
Use of inappropriate summary statistics
Biased interpretation of the statistical results
One should strive to be fair, thorough, objective, and neutral as you
collect, analyze, and present data.
As a consumer of statistics, one should also be aware of the possibility
of unethical behavior by others.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan 22 or 27, 2026 40/61
What is Data Distribution
Data distribution refers to the way in which a set of data points are
spread out or clustered across various values in a dataset.
Data distribution shows how often each different value occurs in a
dataset.
Data distribution visually represents the frequency of different values or
ranges of values in a dataset.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan 22 or 27, 2026 41/61
Distribution Shape
A frequency distribution is a tabular summary of data showing the
number (frequency) of observations in each of several non-overlapping
categories or classes.
Guests staying at a hotel in Downtown San Antonio were asked to rate
the quality of their accommodations:
Figure 8: Hotel Ratings
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan 22 or 27, 2026 42/61
Distribution Shape
The relative frequency of a class is the fraction or proportion of the
total number of data items belonging to the class.
The percent frequency of a class is the relative frequency multiplied
by 100.
Relative Frequency = Frequency
n
Figure 9: Relative Frequency and Percent Frequency Distributions
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan 22 or 27, 2026 43/61
Bar Chart
A bar chart is a graphical display for depicting qualitative data.
A frequency, relative frequency, or percent frequency scale can be
used for the other axis (usually the vertical axis).
Using a bar of fixed width drawn above each class label, we extend
the height appropriately.
The bars are separated to emphasize the fact that each class is a
separate category.
Figure 10: Hotel Quality Ratings Bar Chart
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan 22 or 27, 2026 44/61
Pie Chart
The pie chart is a commonly used graphical display for presenting
relative frequency and percent frequency distributions for categorical
data.
First draw a circle; then use the relative frequencies to subdivide the
circle into sectors that correspond to the relative frequency for each
class.
A class with a relative frequency of 0.25 would consume 0.25(360) =
90 degrees of the circle.
Figure 11: Hotel Quality Ratings Pie Chart

Dot Plot
One of the simplest graphical summaries of data is a dot plot.
A horizontal axis shows the range of data values.
Then each data value is represented by a dot placed above the axis.
Figure 13: Hotel Quality Ratings Pie Chart
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan 22 or 27, 2026 47/61
Histogram
The variable of interest is placed on the horizontal axis.
A rectangle is drawn above each class interval with its height
corresponding to the interval’s frequency, relative frequency, or
percent frequency.
Unlike a bar graph, a histogram has no natural separation between
rectangles of adjacent classes.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan 22 or 27, 2026 48/61

Stem-and-Leaf Display
A stem-and-leaf display shows both the rank order and shape of a
distribution of data.
It is similar to a histogram on its side, but it has the advantage of
showing the actual data values.
The leading digits of each data item are arranged to the left of a
vertical line.
To the right of the vertical line we record the last digit for each item
in rank order.
Each line (row) in the display is referred to as a stem.
Each digit on a stem is a leaf.
Figure 20: Stem and Leaf Plot
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan 22 or 27, 2026 51/61

A scatter diagram is a graphical presentation of the relationship
between two quantitative variables.
One variable is shown on the horizontal axis, and the other variable is
shown on the vertical axis.
The general pattern of the plotted points suggests the overall
relationship between the variables.
A trendline provides an approximation of the relationship.

A side-by-side bar chart is a graphical display for depicting multiple
bar charts on the same display.

Stacked Bar Chart
A stacked bar chart is another way to display and compare two
variables on the same display.
It is a bar chart in which each bar is broken into rectangular segments
of a different color.
If percentage frequencies are displayed, all bars will be of the same
height (or length), extending to the 100% mark.

Data Visualization: Best Practices in Creating
Effective Graphical Displays
Data visualization is the use of graphical displays to summarize and
present information about a data set.
The goal is to communicate as effectively and clearly as possible, the
key information about the data.
Give the display a clear and concise title.
Keep the display simple.
Clearly label each axis and provide the units of measure.
If colors are used, make sure they are distinct.
If multiple colors or line types are used, provide a legend.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan 22 or 27, 2026 57/61

Displays used to show the distribution of data:
Bar Chart to show the frequency distribution and relative frequency
distribution for categorical data
Pie Chart to show the relative frequency and percent frequency for
categorical data
Dot Plot to show the distribution for quantitative data over the entire
range of the data
Histogram to show the frequency distribution for quantitative data over
a set of class intervals
Stem-and-Leaf Display to show both the rank order and shape of the
distribution for quantitative data

What is Hypothesis Testing? And Why Do We
Need It?
We are going to make guesses/predictions all the time
Some are right and some are wrong
2 types of wrong answers
Set up the boundaries for the guess
If your guess is within the limits, it’s a correct guess
If your guess if out of the limits, it’s a false/wrong guess
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/01/2024 6/28
Hypothesis Testing
Hypothesis testing can be used to determine whether a statement
about the value of a population parameter should or should not be
rejected or believed to be true/false
The null hypothesis, denoted byH0 , is atentative assumption about
a population parameter
The alternative hypothesis, denoted byHa, is the opposite of what is
stated in the null hypothesis
The hypothesis testing procedure uses data from a sample to test the
two competing statements indicated byH0 and Ha
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/01/2024 7/28
Developing Null and Alternative Hypotheses
It could be tricky to understand the context of the situation and
structure the hypotheses appropriately so that the test conclusion
provides the desired information.
All hypothesis testing applications involvecollecting a sampleand
using the sample results to provide evidence for drawing a conclusion.
Good questions to consider when formulating the null and alternative
hypotheses are:
What is the purpose of collecting the sample?
What conclusions are we hoping to make?
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/01/2024 8/28
Developing Null and Alternative Hypotheses -
Example
When an attempt is being made to gather evidence in support of a
hypothesis, it is often best to begin with the alternative hypothesis
and make it the conclusion that the researcher hopes to support.
The conclusion that the research hypothesis is true is made if the
sample data provide sufficient evidence to show that the null
hypothesis can be rejected.
Example: A new fuel injection unit being developed is believed to
provide an MPG of more than 24 MPG.
We set the population mean miles per gallonHa :µ>24 as the
alternative hypothesis
And set the null hypothesis asH0 :µ≤24
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/01/2024 9/28
Developing Null and Alternative Hypotheses -
Example
We might begin with a belief or assumption that a statement about
the value of a population parameter is true.
We then use a hypothesis test to challenge the assumption and
determine if there is statistical evidence to conclude that the
assumption is incorrect.
Example: The label on an Ozarka water bottle states that it contains
16.9 fluid ounces.
We assume the label correct, provided the population mean filling
weight,µ, is 16.9 fluid ounces, and set it as the null hypothesis, or
H0 :µ= 16.9
Then, we challenge the assumption in the alternative hypothesis
Ha :µ̸= 16.9
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/01/2024 10/28
Summary of Forms for Null and Alternative
Hypotheses
Rule 1: The equality part of the hypotheses always appears in the null
hypothesis.
Rule 2: A hypothesis test about the value of a population meanµ
must take one of the followingTHREE forms, whereµ0 is the
hypothesized value of the population mean.
One-tailed (Lower-tail): H0 :µ≥µ0 vs. Ha :µ<µ0
One-tailed (Upper-tail): H0 :µ≤µ0 vs. Ha :µ>µ0
Two-tailed (Both Upper and Lower):H0 :µ=µ0 vs. Ha :µ̸=µ0
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/01/2024 11/28
Summary of Forms for Null and Alternative
Hypotheses
In this chapter, we show how statistical inference from sample data
and results can be used together with hypothesis testing to determine
whether a statement about thevalue of a population parameter
should or should not be rejected.
We quantify thefavor or support, if the sample results support or
favorite Ha,
We reject H0
Otherwise, we do not rejectH0
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/01/2024 12/28
We Are Humans, We Make Mistakes/Wrong
Guesses.
Because hypothesis tests are based on sample data, we must allow for
the possibility of errors.
There are two types of error that can be made in hypothesis tests:
Type I and Type II errors.
Figure 1: Error Types
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/01/2024 13/28
Type I and Type II Errors
Figure 2: Error Types
Figure 3: Correct Predictions and
Error Types
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/01/2024 14/28
Type I and Type II Errors
Type I Error
A Type I error is rejectingH0 when it is true.
The probability of making a Type I error when the null hypothesis is
true as an equality is called the level of significance.
Applications of hypothesis testing that only control for the Type I error
are often called significance tests.
Type II Error
A Type II error is acceptingH0 when it is false.
Statisticians avoid the risk of making a Type II error by using “do not
reject H0” rather than “acceptH0”. - SNEAKY!!
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/01/2024 15/28
Example of Type I and Type II Errors
Recall the hypothesis testing example about the new fuel injection
unit. Imagine you’ve developed a new fuel system for cars, and you
believe it makes cars more fuel-efficient than the current standard.
You decide to test if this new system really improves the car’s miles
per gallon (MPG) rating compared to the usual 24 MPG benchmark.
H0 :µ≤24
Ha :µ>24 (1)
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/01/2024 16/28
Example of Type I and Type II Errors
Type I Error: This is like sounding a false alarm. It’s when you
conclude that your new fuel system does indeed improve the car’s
MPG (makes it better than 24 MPG), but in reality, it doesn’t — it’s
just the same as the old system. So, you mistakenly think you’ve
made an improvement when you haven’t.
Type II Error: This is like missing something important. It’s when you
decide that your new fuel system doesn’t make any difference (so,
MPG is 24 or worse), but in fact, it actually does improve the MPG.
So, you’ve overlooked a real improvement, thinking your system
didn’t make the car more fuel-efficient when it really did.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/01/2024 17/28
Example of Type I and Type II Errors
In summary, a Type I error is thinking you’ve achieved something
special when you haven’t, and a Type II error is failing to recognize an
actual achievement, thinking nothing has changed.
Type I Error: Thinking your new system works (improves MPG) when
it doesn’t.
Type II Error: Not realizing your new system works (actually improves
MPG) when it does.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/01/2024 18/28
One-Tailed Test About a Population Mean
One-tailed tests about a population mean take one of the following
two forms.
Lower Tail Test
H0 :µ≥µ0
Ha :µ<µ0
(2)
One-tailed tests about a population mean take one of the following
two forms.
Upper Tail Test
H0 :µ≤µ0
Ha :µ>µ0
(3)
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/01/2024 19/28
One-Tailed Test About a Population Mean -
Example
Consider the example of the Federal Trade Commission (FTC) testing
a Hilltop Coffee’s claim that its large can contains at least 3 pounds
of coffee.
The FTC assumes Hilltop Coffee’s claim to be correct (µ≥3 pounds)
and sets the alternative hypothesis to challenge the claim, using the
lower tail test,µ<3 pounds. Thus, we write:
H0 :µ≥3
Ha :µ<3 (4)
The FTC is willing to take a 1% risk of making an error on its testing
of Hilltop Coffee’s claim.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/01/2024 20/28
Test Statistic for the Hilltop Coffee Example
Suppose a sample ofn = 36 cans of coffee is selected, and the sample
mean ¯x is computed as an estimate of the population meanµ.
Also, previous FTC tests show that the population of filling weights
can be assumed normally distributed with a known standard deviation
of valueσ= 0.18
We can write the sampling distribution of¯x as normally distributed,
centered around the hypothesized mean,µ=µ0 = 3, and with
standard error:
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/01/2024 21/28
Test Statistic for the Hilltop Coffee Example
For the test statistic of a hypothesis test about a population mean:σ
known, we can use the standard normal random variable,z. Formally,
we write:
Figure 4: Sampling Distribution for the Coffee Example
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/01/2024 22/28
p-Value Approach to a Lower Tail Test About a
Population Mean: σ Known
A p-value is a probability, computed using the test statistic, that
measures the support provided by the sample for the null hypothesis.
Suppose the sample ofn = 36 coffee cans revealed¯x = 2.92 pounds.
Then, the test statistic is
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/01/2024 23/28
p-Value Approach to a Lower Tail Test About a
Population Mean: σ Known
For a lower tail test, the p-value is the probability thatz is less than
or equal to−2.67.
p−value = P(z≤−2.67) = 0.0038
This p-value indicates a small probability of obtaining a sample mean
of ¯x = 2.92 or smaller when sampling from a population withµ= 3.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/01/2024 24/28
Rejection Rule Using p-Value
In the previous slide, we concluded that the calculated p-value does
not provide much support for the null hypothesis, but is it small
enough to cause us to rejectH0?
The answer depends upon the level of significanceαfor the test:
If a p-value is less than or equal to the level of significanceα, the value
of the test statistic is in the rejection region.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/01/2024 25/28
Rejection Rule #1: Using p-Value
Because the FTC stated to be willing to take a 1% risk of making a
Type I error, we set the significance levelα= 0.01.
We write the rejection rule using the p-value as:
Reject H0 if p-value≤α
With the p-value = 0.0038 being less than or equal toα= 0.01, we
reject H0.
Therefore, the FTC finds sufficient statistical evidence to conclude at
the 0.01 level of significance that the filling weight of Hilltop Coffee
cans is less than 3 pounds.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/01/2024 26/28
Rejection Rule #2: Using Critical Value
We can use the standard normal probability distribution to find thez
value with an area ofαin the lower (or upper) tail of the distribution.
The value of the test statistic that establishes the boundary of the
rejection region is called the critical value for the test.
The rejection rule for a one-tailed test using the critical value is:
Lower Tail: RejectH0 if z≤zα
Upper Tail: RejectH0 if z≥zα
zα is such thatP(z≤zα) =α.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/01/2024 27/28
Rejection Rule #2: Using Critical Value
In the Hilltop Coffee case, whenα= 0.01, zα =−2.33.
The rejection rule for a lower tail test becomes
Reject H0 if z≤−2.33, and becausez =−2.67, we rejectH0.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/01/2024 28/28

Point Estimation
Point estimation is a form of statistical inference
In point estimation we use the data from the sample to compute a
value of a sample statistic that serves as an estimate of a population
parameter
We refer to¯x as the point estimator of the population meanµ
s is the point estimator of the population standard deviationσ
¯p is the point estimator of the population proportionp

Central Limit Theorem(CLT)
Z Distribution/Score helps turn any non-standard normal distributions
into standard normal distribution
CLT helps turn any distributions into standard normal distribution!!!
Figure 8: Why Is CLT So Useful
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/02/2025 20/29
Central Limit Theorem(CLT)
As the sample size gets large enough, the sampling distribution
becomes almost normal regardless of shape of population
This magic trick is true all the time!
Why is CLT useful?
It allows the use of normal distribution assumptions for sample means,
regardless of the original data’s distribution. This simplification enables
easier statistical analysis, hypothesis testing, and confidence interval
construction
It’s widely applicable across various fields for making informed decisions
based on sample data
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/02/2025 21/29
Central Limit Theorem(CLT)
Figure 9: Central Limit Theorem
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/02/2025 22/29
So Many Different Sample Sizes, How Large is
Large Enough?
For most distributions,n> 25 is the Golden Rule will give a sampling
distribution that is nearly normal
1 Check if the sample size is lager than 25. CLT automatically becomes
TRUE if the sample size is larger than 25
2 Once confirmed normal, you can applyZ = x−µ
σ to find the probability
For normal population distributions, the sampling distribution of the
mean is always normally distributed
Sampling distribution properties:
Central Tendency:µ¯X =µ
Variation: σ¯X = σ√n
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/02/2025 23/29
Central Limit Theorem(CLT) Example
You are managing a company that sells handmade candles
Where the average production cost per candle is $8(µ= 8) with a
variability in cost (standard deviation) of $3(σ= 3). The company
decides to take a random sample of 36 candles(n = 36) to analyze
production expenses.
The question becomes: What is the probability that the average
production cost for this sample of candles is between $7.8 and $8.2?
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/02/2025 24/29
Central Limit Theorem(CLT)
Central Limit Theorem(CLT) is like an universal key that can unlock
any different types of distributions and predict the probability with
the help of normal distribution
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/02/2025 25/29
Central Limit Theorem(CLT)
By leveraging the CLT, businesses can apply statistical methods
confidently, enhancing decision-making, improving products and
services, and ultimately achieving strategic goals with greater
efficiency.
Quality Control: In manufacturing, CLT helps assess the quality of
products. By sampling a few products from a production line,
companies can predict the overall quality and make adjustments
without inspecting every item.
Customer Satisfaction: Businesses can survey a sample of customers
about their satisfaction levels. CLT assures that even if individual
opinions are varied, the average satisfaction score from multiple
samples will form a normal distribution, helping to gauge overall
customer sentiment accurately.
Financial Forecasting: For investment analysis, CLT helps in predicting
the average returns of a portfolio. By understanding the distribution of
average returns, businesses can assess risk and make informed
investment decisions.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/02/2025 26/29
Central Limit Theorem Example(Team Presentation)
The Bureau of Transportation Statistics reports that the mean airfare for
flights departing from San Antonio International Airport during the first
three months of 2020 was $279.19. Assume the standard deviation for this
population of fares is known to be $80. Suppose a random sample of 60
flights departing from San Antonio International Airport during the first
three months of 2022 is taken.
a. If the mean and standard deviation of the population of airfares for
flights departing from the airport didn’t changed between the first
three months of 2021 and the first three months of 2022, what is the
probability the sample mean will be within $20 of the population
mean cost per flight?

Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/17/2025 3 / 23
Introduction
In this chapter, we study continuous random variables and discuss
THREE continuous probability distributions:
The continuous uniform,
The exponential,
The normal.
Because of its wide applicability and its extensive use in statistical
inference in business management, economics and engineering, the
normal distribution is the big boss.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/17/2025 4 / 23
Introduction
Probabilities are computed differently for discrete and continuous
random variables.
With discrete random variables, the probability functionf (x ) provides
the probability that the random variable assumes a particular value.
With continuous random variables,
The probability that x assumes a particular value is zero, and a value in
a given interval is the corresponding area under the graph of f (x );
f (x ) is called the Probability Density Function(PDF).
Continuous probability distribution: A probability distribution in which
the continuous random variablex can take on any value, and there are
infinite values that X could assume.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/17/2025 5 / 23
Why Called PDF?
What’s the area of a square?
What’s the area of a rectangle?
What’s the area of a circle?

Why Called PDF?
What’s the area of the following shape?
Figure 1: A Weird Continuous Distribution
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/17/2025 7 / 23
Continuous Uniform Distribution
A random variable is described by a uniform probability distribution
whenever the probability is proportional to the interval’s length.
In English, The probability density function for the continuous uniform
distribution is constant across the entire range.
The uniform probability density function is:
f (x ) =
{ 1
b−a for a≤x≤b
0 elsewhere (1)
Where
a = smallest value the variablex can assume
b = largest value the variablex can assume
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/17/2025 8 / 23
Continuous Uniform Distribution Examples
New Product Pricing : When a company launches a new product and
has limited historical pricing data, it might assume that the acceptable
price range to consumers is uniformly distributed. For example, if the
company believes consumers will pay between $50 and $100 for the
product, every price within this range is considered equally likely
until further market research is conducted .
Demand Forecasting During a Limited-Time Event : For events
with little historical data, such as the annual sale of a small business,
the expected sales per day could be modeled as uniformly distributed
within a reasonable range, assuming each day of the event is equally
promoted and accessible.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/17/2025 9 / 23
Uniform Distribution
For an uniform continuous probability distribution
The expected value is:
E (x ) = a + b
2
The variance is:
Var(x ) = (b−a)2
12
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/17/2025 10 / 23
Area as a Measure of Probability
The flight time of an airplane traveling from Chicago to New York can
be described by a continuous random variable
The historic data shows that all non-delayed flights take 120 to 140
minutes.
The flight time is uniformly distributed between 120 and 140 minutes.
That is, the probability of the flight time being within any 1-minute
interval between 120 and 140 minutes is equal for each minute.
Thus, the random variablex is said to have the following uniform
probability distribution.
f (x ) =
{ 1
140−120 for 120≤x≤140
0 elsewhere (2)

f (x )≥0 for any value ofx
The area under the graph off (x ) is equal to 1
For example, in the airplane traveling time problem seen in the
previous slide
P(120≤x≤140) = 1
20×(140−120) = 1

Exponential Probability Distribution
The exponential probability distribution is used for random variables
that describe the length of the interval between occurrences, such as
the time between arrivals at a hospital emergency room.
The exponential probability density function is
f (x ) = 1
µe− x
µ for x≥0
- Whereµis the expected value or mean.
Properties: under the exponential distribution, the mean(µ) and
standard deviation(σ) are the same. Variance isσ2.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/17/2025 15 / 23

probability that the random variableX is a certain valuex. It is
obtained by integrating the PDF from 0 tox:
F (x ) = P(X ) =

0
f (x )dx
Take the derivative of the CDF, you will get
P(X ) = e− x

Computing Probabilities for the Exponential
Distribution
To compute the probability of an event that follows an exponential
distribution, do NOT useF (x ) or f (x ), use P(x ) instead.
To compute exponential probabilities, we use this formula for the
cumulative probability of obtaining a value for the exponential random
variable that is less than or equal to some specific value denoted byx.
P(X ) = 1−e− x
µ

Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan. 20 or 21, 2026 5/32
Probability for Business
Definition: Business probability involves the study and application of
probability theory to business situations.
It deals with the likelihood of different outcomes or events
occurring in the business environment.
In the world of probability, we only talk about likelihood, and there is
no such thing as extreme as 100% or 0%.
Focus: The primary focus is on forecasting and estimating the
likelihood of future events based on certain assumptions.
It’s about understanding and quantifying uncertainty or the
random events.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan. 20 or 21, 2026 6/32
Probability for Business(cont.)
Applications:
Risk Assessment: Estimating the likelihood of various business risks,
such as market downturns or project failures.
Financial Modeling: Predicting future financial outcomes, like cash flow
or stock prices, based on probabilistic models.
Decision Analysis: Helping in making decisions under uncertainty by
evaluating the probabilities of different outcomes.
Techniques Used: Probability distributions (normal, binomial,
Poisson, etc.), risk analysis models, Monte Carlo simulations, etc.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan. 20 or 21, 2026 7/32
Statistics for Business
Definition: Business statistics involves the use of statistical methods
to analyze data relevant to business activities.
It encompasses the collection, analysis, interpretation, and
presentation of business data.
Focus: The focus is on understanding and drawing inferences from
existing data.
It involves using statistical tools to make sense of historical data
and to inform business decisions.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan. 20 or 21, 2026 8/32
Statistics for Business (cont.)
Applications:
Market Analysis: Analyzing market trends and consumer behaviors
through statistical methods.
Quality Control: Using statistical process control to maintain and
improve product quality.
Performance Measurement: Evaluating business performance through
key statistical indicators.
Techniques Used: Descriptive statistics (mean, median, mode, etc.),
inferential statistics (hypothesis testing, regression analysis), time
series analysis, etc.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan. 20 or 21, 2026 9/32
The Main Difference Between Probability and
Statistics
Both are very closely related cousins.
Main difference
Probability is more about predicting future events and quantifying
uncertainty.
Intuitive way to understand “how much” an uncertainty would be.
Statistics is more about analyzing past and present data to make
informed business decisions.
We need to understand the past and the present to predict the future.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business Jan. 20 or 21, 2026 10/32
Decision Making for Public Health
Health care providers create and use statistical/probabilistic methods
to model uncertainty and make better decisions.
Decision-making assistant.
Health officials can predict how quickly an infectious disease is likely to
spread.
Probability models help in understanding the likelihood of disease
transmission under different scenarios.
Guiding decisions about lockdowns, social distancing, and resource
allocation for healthcare.
Knowing the consequences of a new drug or a new treatment faster.
Both tools play a crucial role in new drug development by guiding the
design and analysis of clinical trials.
Help ensure that new medications are both safe and effective.
Help determine the probability of a drug’s effectiveness compared to an
existing drug or another candidate drug.
Help assess the likelihood or probability and severity of potential side
effects and other issues.

Correlation vs. Causation
Correlation doesn’t mean causation.
Causation often implies some levels of correlation, but the strength
isn’t guaranteed/confirmed.

Correlation vs. Causation
Did you make the correct conclusion after seeing the incomplete
table?

Just because two things happen together (Correlation) doesn’t mean
one caused the other (Causation). Usually, there is a third or more,
hidden factor(s) pulling the strings.

How is Probability Calculated?

Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 7/77
Definitions
Probability is a numerical measure of the likelihood that an event will
occur. Probability values are always assigned on a scale from 0 to 1:
A probability near zero indicates an event is quite unlikely to occur.
A probability near one indicates an event is almost certain to occur.
Other probabilities between 0 and 1 represent degrees of likelihood that
an event will occur.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 8/77
Randomness
Randomness refers to the lack of pattern or predictability in events. A
random sequence of events has no order and does not follow some
pattern or combination.
Rolling a die, flipping a coin, weather patterns, stock market
fluctuations
Want to try the best to have some control over the randomnesses
Probability and statistics are the tools to understand those
randomnesses
Understanding randomness is key to interpreting data, predicting
phenomena, and designing systems in various scientific and practical
fields.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 9/77
Random Experiment
A Random experiment is a process that generates experimental
outcomes, and possesses the following properties
The experimental outcomes are well-defined and may also be listed
prior to conducting the experiment.
On any single repetition (trial) of the experiment, one and only one of
the possible experimental outcomes will occur.
The experimental outcome that occurs on any trial is determined solely
by chance.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 10/77
Statistical Experiments vs. Other Physical Science
Experiments
In statistics, the notion of an experiment differs somewhat from the
notion of an experiment in the physical sciences.
In the physical sciences, researchers usually conduct an experiment in a
laboratory or a controlled environment in order to learn about cause
and effect.
In statistical experiments, probability determines outcomes.
Even though the experiment is repeated in exactly the same way, an
entirely different outcome may occur.
Because of this influence of probability on the outcome, the
experiments of statistics are sometimes called random experiments.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 11/77
Random Experiment and Sample Space
An experimental outcome is also called asample point. The sample
space, S, for an experiment is the set of all experimental outcomes.
Example: consider the random experiment of tossing a coin.
The experimental outcomes (sample points) areHead and Tail.
The sample space is:s ={Head, Tail}

Counting Rule for Multiple-Step Experiments
A multiple-step experiment can be described as a sequence ofk steps
with n1 possible outcomes on the first step,n2 possible outcomes on
the second step, and so on. (n1,n2,n3, ...,nk)
As an example, consider the random experiment of tossing two coins,
one at a time. The first tossed coin hasn1 = 2 outcomes, and the
second tossed coin has alson2 = 2 outcomes.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 15/77
Counting Rule for Multiple-Step Experiments
From the counting rule, we have:n1∗n2 = 2∗2 = 4 experimental
outcomes
The sample space is:S = (H,H),(H,T ),(T ,H),(T ,T )
The tree diagram for the two-coin toss shown a graphical
representation that helps visualizing a multiple-step experiment.
Figure 1: Tree Diagram for Two-Coin Tossing

Assigning Probabilities
The basic requirements for assigning probabilities are
The probability assigned to each experimental outcome must be
between 0 and 1, inclusively.
The sum of the probabilities for all experimental outcomes must equal
1.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 29/77
Assigning Probabilities
Three methods of assigning probabilities:
The classical method, is based on the assumption of equally likely
outcomes.
The empirical method, based on relative frequencies from experimental
or historical data.
The subjective method, based on experience or intuition when no data
are available.

Events
An event is a collection of sample points.
If we can identify all the sample points of an experiment and assign a
probability to each, we can compute the probability of an event.
1604 Project was an example
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 34/77
Events
Texas DoT was interested in the probability that the project is
completed in 10 months or less.
From the table in the previous slide, we see that there are six sample
points that provide a project completion time of 10 months or less.
Let C denote the event that the project is completed in 10 months or
less. Thus, we have:
C = {(2, 6), (2, 7), (2, 8), (3, 6), (3, 7), (4, 6)}
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 35/77
Events
Some other possible events may be:

M, the event that the project will complete in more than 10 months:
M ={(3,8),(4,7),(4,8)}
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 36/77
Probability of An Event
The probability of an event is equal to the sum of the probabilities of
the sample points in the event.
We can calculate the probability that the 1604 project is completed in
10 months or lessby adding the probabilities of the six sample
points belonging to eventC.
P(C) = P(2,6) + P(2,7) + P(2,8) + P(3,6) + P(3,7) + P(4,6)
P(C) = 0.15 +0.15 +0.05 +0.10 +0.20 +0.05 = 0.7
Similarly, the probability that the 1604 project is completed inless
than 10 monthsis
P(L) = P(2,6) + P(2,7) + P(3,6) = 0.15 +0.15 +0.10 = 0.4
And the probability that the 1604 project is completed inmore than
10 monthsis
P(M) = P(3,8) + P(4,7) + P(4,8) = 0.05 +0.10 +0.15 = 0.3

Some Basic Relationships of Probability
In an experiment with a large number of sample points, the
identification of all the sample points, as well as the determination of
their associated probabilities, can become an extremely cumbersome
task.
In this section, we present some basic probability relationships that
can be used to compute the probability of an event without
knowledge of all the sample point probabilities.
The complement of an event
The union of two events
The intersection of two events
The addition law
The multiplication law

Some Basic Relationships of Probability
In an experiment with a large number of sample points, the
identification of all the sample points, as well as the determination of
their associated probabilities, can become an extremely cumbersome
task.
In this section, we present some basic probability relationships that
can be used to compute the probability of an event without
knowledge of all the sample point probabilities.
The complement of an event
The union of two events
The intersection of two events
The addition law
The multiplication law
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 41/77
Complement of an Event
The complement of eventA is defined as the event consisting of all
sample points that are not inA
The complement ofA is denoted byAC
In any probability application, either eventA or its complementAC
must occur, as shown in the Venn diagram
Figure 5: Venn Diagram
Therefore, we have
P(A) + P(AC ) = 1 or P(A) = 1−P(AC )
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 42/77
Complement of an Event
The complement event of an eventA can also be denoted as¯A, which
consists of all outcomes that are not in A. Namely event¯A occurs if A
does not occur.
Example: Roll a die:E1 ={1,3,5}. Then ¯E1 ={2,4,6}
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 43/77
Union of Two Events
The union of eventsA and B is the event containing all sample points
that are inA and B or both.
The union of eventsA and B is denoted byA∪B.
Note that the two circles in the Venn diagram overlap, indicating that
some sample points are contained in bothA and B .
Figure 6: Venn Diagram - Union
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 44/77
Union of Two Events
Union: The union event of two eventsA and B is denoted asA∪B,
which consists of all outcomes in eitherA or B or in both. Namely
event A∪B occurs if eitherA or B occurs.
Example: Roll a die:E1 ={1,3,5}and E2 ={1,2,3}. Then
E1∪E2 ={1,2,3,5}
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 45/77
Intersection of Two Events
The intersection of eventsA and B is the event containing all sample
points belonging to bothA and B. The intersection of eventsA and
B is denoted byA∩B.
Note that the area in the Venn diagram where the two circles overlap
is the intersection.
The intersection contains all the sample points that are in bothA and
B.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 46/77
Intersection of Two Events
The intersection event of two eventsA and B is denoted asA∩B,
which consists of all outcomes in bothA and B or in both.
A∩B occurs if bothA and B occur.
Example: Roll a die:E1 ={1,3,5}and E2 ={1,2,3}, then
E1∩E2 ={1,3}
Two eventsA and B are mutually exclusive ifA∩B =∅.
Figure 7: Venn Diagram - Intersection
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 47/77
Addition Law
The addition law provides a way to compute the probability of the
union of two events:
P(A∪B) = P(A) + P(B)−P(A∩B)
To understand the addition law intuitively, consider the following:
The sample points in the intersectionA∩B are in bothA and B.
When we computeP(A) + P(B)we are in effect counting each of the
sample points inA∩B twice.
We correct for this overcounting by subtractingP(A∩B)
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 48/77
Addition Law: Example
Toss two fair coins in very trial and assume that the four possible
outcomes S ={HH,HT ,TH,TT}are equally likely to happen.
Let A ={HH,HT}be the event that the first coin falls head, and
B ={HH,TH}be the event that the second coin falls head.
Problem: Find the probability that either the first coin falls head or
the second coin falls head?
Solution:
P(A∪B) = P(A) + P(B)−P(A∩B) = 1
2 + 1
2−1
4 = 3
4
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 49/77
An Application of the Addition Law
Consider a group of 50 software engineers who work in online banking.
At the end of an evaluation period, a bank manager found that
5 engineers completed work late (eventL), or P(L) = 5/50 = 0.10
6 engineers produced code that contains errors (eventE), or
P(E) = 6/50 = 0.12
2 engineers completed work late and produced code that contains
errors (event L∩E), or P(L∩E) = 2/50 = 0.04
The probability that an engineer completed work late or produced
code that contains errors (eventL∪E) can be calculated using the
addition law as
P(L∪E) = P(L) + P(E)−P(L∩E) = 0.10 +0.12−0.04 = 0.18
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 50/77
Mutually Exclusive Events
Two events are said to be mutually exclusive when they have no
sample points in common.
In other words, eventsA and B are mutually exclusive if, when one
event occurs, the other cannot occur.
Thus, the intersection ofA and B does not contain any points, and
P(A∩B) = 0.
When two events are mutually exclusive, the addition law simplifies to
P(A∪B) = P(A) + P(B)
Figure 8: Venn Diagram - Mutually Exclusive Events
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 51/77
Conditional Probability
We use the notationP(A|B) to denote the conditional probability of
event A, given that eventB has occurred.
We calculate the probability of eventA given B as
P(A|B) = P(A∩B)
P(B)
Figure 9: Venn Diagram - Conditional Events
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 52/77
Conditional Probability
We calculate the probability of eventB given A as
P(B|A) = P(A∩B)
P(A)
Therefore, P(A|B) and P(B|A) are NOT the same thing.

The police force of a major metropolitan area in the eastern U.S.

self-identified females.
The specific breakdown of the promotions of 288 male and 36 female
police officers over the past two years is summarized in the

After reviewing the promotion record, a committee of female officers
raised a discrimination case on the basis of the low promotion of
female officers when compared to male officers.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 54/77
A Discrimination Case on the Promotion of Police
Officers
The police administration argued that the lower number of
promotions for female officers was due to the relatively lower number
of female officers in the police force.
Figure 10: Police Promotion Data
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 55/77
Joint Probability Table
Dividing the data values in the crosstabulation by the total of 1,200
officers enables us to summarize the available information on
promotions with a joint probability table.
In the body of the table, we have the joint probabilities, which are the
intersection probabilities of the events of whether a police officer was
promoted A or not AC with whether the police officer is maleM or
female F.
The joint probability that an officer was promotedA and is a maleM
P(A∩M) = 288/1200 = 0.24
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 56/77
Joint Probability Table
On the margins of the table, we have the marginal probabilities,
providing the probabilities of each separate event.
The probability that an officer is maleM
P(M) = 960/1200 = 0.80
Figure 11: Police Promotion Data and Joint Probabilities
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 57/77
Conditional Probability Analysis
We note that the marginal probabilities are found by summing the
joint probabilities in the corresponding row or column of the joint
probability table.
P(A) = P(A∩M) + P(A∩F ) = 0.24 +0.03 = 0.27
The conditional probability that an officer was promotedA given that
the officer is maleM
P(A|M) = P(A∩M)
P(M) = 288/1200
960/1200 = 288
960 = 0.30
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 58/77
Conditional Probability Analysis
The conditional probability that an officer was promotedA given that
the officer is femaleF
P(A|F ) = P(A∩F )
P(F ) = 36/1200
240/1200 = 36
240 = 0.15
Thus, because P(A|M) > P(A|F ), or 0.3 > 0.15, the analysis
supports the female officers’ argument.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 59/77
Independent Events
Two events A and B are independent if
P(A|B) = P(A) or P(B|A) = P(B)
Otherwise, the events are dependent if
P(A|B)̸= P(A) or P(B|A)̸= P(B)
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 60/77
Independent Events vs. Mutually Exclusive Events
Mutually exclusive events could refer to choosing between two
investment options
Such as investing in either stocks or bonds with a limited budget, where
selecting one option precludes investing in the other at the same time.
Independent events might involve the scenario where the performance
of a stock in the technology sector is unaffected by a change in
interest rates
Meaning the stock’s performance and the interest rate movement do
not influence each other’s outcomes.
Figure 12: Mutually Exclusive vs. Independent Events
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 61/77
Independent Events
In the analysis of the promotion of police officers, we found
P(A) = 0.27,P(A|M) = 0.30,P(A|F ) = 0.15
Because P(A|M)̸= P(A), that is, the probability of whether an
officer is promotedA is altered or affected by knowing whether the
officer is maleM, we conclude that the eventsA and M are
dependent or not independent.
Similarly, becauseP(A|F )̸= P(A), we would say that eventsA and F
are also dependent.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 62/77
Independent Events
A quick note:
Do not confuse the notion of mutually exclusive events with that of
independent events.
Two events with nonzero probabilities cannot be both mutually
exclusive and independent.
If one mutually exclusive event is known to occur, the other cannot
occur.
Thus, the probability of the other event occurring is reduced to 0.
Therefore, the two events are dependent.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 63/77
Multiplication Law
The multiplication law is based on the definition of conditional
probability, and it provides a way to compute the probability of the
intersection of two events
P(A∩B) = P(A|B)P(B) or P(B∩A) = P(B|A)P(A)
In the case of independent eventsP(A|B) = P(A) or P(B|A) = P(B),
and the multiplication law for independent eventsbecomes
P(A∩B) = P(A)P(B) or P(B∩A) = P(B)P(A)
Therefore, two eventsA and B are dependent when
P(A∩B)̸= P(A)P(B)
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 64/77
Multiplication Law
Example: Draw three cardsWITH replacement i.e., draw one card,
look at it, p ut it back, and re p eat twice more.
Problem: Find the probability of drawing 3 Queens in a row
Solution: Let Qi(i = 1,2,3) be the event thati th draw gives you a
Queen. Then the three events are independent of each other.
P(Q1∩Q2∩Q3) = P(Q1)P(Q2)P(Q3) = 4
52×4
52×4
52 = 0.00046
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 65/77
Multiplication Law
Example: Draw three cardsWITHOUT replacement i.e., draw one
card, look at it, keep it, and repeat for 2 more times
Problem: Find the probability of drawing 3 Queens in a row
Solution: Let Qi(i = 1,2,3) be the event thati th draw gives you a
Queen. Then the three events are dependent of each other.
P(Q1∩Q2∩Q3) = P(Q1)P(Q2|Q1)P(Q3|Q1∩Q2) = 4
52×3
51×2
50 = 0.00018
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 66/77
Prior and Posterior Probabilities
Often, we begin probability analysis with initial orprior probabilities.
Then, from a sample, special report, or a product test we obtain some
additional information.
Given this information, we calculate revised orposterior probabilities.
Bayes’ theorem provides the means for revising the prior probabilities.
Figure 13: Bayes’ Process
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 67/77
Bayes’ Theorem
Given an event B, and a set of mutually exclusive and exhaustive
events A1,A2, ...,An, and we know the prior probabilities
P(A1),P(A2), ...,P(An). Then the posterior is:
P(A1|B) = P(B|A1)P(A1)
P(B|A1)P(A1) + ...+ P(B|An)P(An)
Figure 14: Bayes’ Theorem
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 68/77
Bayes’ Theorem: Example
Example: 25 percent of residents in an area leaves their garage doors
open when they left their home. The chances of being raided are 5
percent and 1 percent for those who leave their doors open, and who
do not leave their doors open, respectively.
Problem: Suppose one resident was robbed, what is the probability
that he or she originally left his or her door open?
Solution: Let B = robbed residents,A1 = open doors residents,A2
= close door residents. We know that
P(A1) = 0.25,P(A2) = 0.75,P(B|A1) = 0.05,P(B|A2) = 0.01.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 69/77
Bayes’ Theorem: Example
Figure 15: Bayes’ Theorem: Tree Diagram
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 70/77
Bayes’ Theorem: Example
Therefore
P(A1|B) = P(B|A1)P(A1)
P(B|A1)P(A1) + P(B|A2)P(A2)
P(A1|B) = 0.05(0.25)
0.05(0.25) +0.01(0.75)
P(A1|B) = 0.0125
0.02 = 0.625
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 71/77

Similarly, we cal also calcualte
P(A2|B) = 0.01(0.75)
0.05(0.25) +0.01(0.75)
P(A2|B) = 0.0075
0.02 = 0.375
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 72/77
Bayes’ Theorem: Another Example
Example: An insurance company classifies drivers as good, medium,
or poor risks. Drivers who apply to for insurance fall into these three
groups in the proportions: 30%, 50%, and 20%, respectively. The
probabilities of a good-risk, medium-risk, and poor-risk drivers will
have an accident are 0.01, 0.03, and 0.10, respectively.
Problem: Suppose the company sells Mr. Brophy an insurance policy
and he has an accident. What is the probability that Mr. Brophy was
a good-risk driver?
Solution: Let B = accident drivers,A1 = good drivers,A2 = medium
drivers, and A3 = poor drivers. We know thatP(A1) = 0.3,P(A2) =
0.5,P(A3) = 0.2; P(B|A1) = 0.01,P(B|A2) = 0.03,P(B|A3) = 0.10.

0.01(0.3) +0.03(0.5) +0.10(0.2)

0.038 = 0.079
