BAT2301 - Statistics for Business
Lecture 9 Introduction to Discrete Probability Distributions - 2
Ian Liu
Trinity University
03/03/2025
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/03/2025 1/26
What Is Binomial Distribution? And When To Use
It?
A binomial distributionis a statistical tool to model the number of
successes in a fixed number of trials (or experiments) where each trial
has only two possible outcomes: success or failure.
Think of it as flipping a coin multiple times and counting how many
heads (successes) you get.
Use the binomial distribution when:
You want to model the number of times an event occurs (like getting
heads) in a fixed number of attempts (like flipping a coin).
You’re dealing with situations where there are only two possible
outcomes (success or failure) for each attempt.
You know the probability of success for each trial and want to find out
the likelihood of getting a specific number of successes.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/03/2025 2/26
Difference Between Uniform Distribution and
Binomial Distribution
Binomial Distribution: Used when you have a fixed number of trials,
each with two possible outcomes (success or failure). You’re
interested in the number of successes.
Example: Flipping a coin 10 times and counting how many heads
(successes) you get.
Uniform Distribution: Used when all outcomes are equally likely.
Every possible outcome has the same probability.
Example: Rolling a fair die, where each of the six faces (1, 2, 3, 4, 5,
or 6) has an equal chance of showing up.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/03/2025 3/26
The Binomial Experiment
A binomial experiment has the following four properties.
1 The experiment consists of a sequence ofn identical trials.
2 Two outcomes, success and failure, are possible in each trial.
3 The stationarity assumption: the probability of a success, denoted by
p, and the probability of a failure, denoted by 1−p, do not change
from trial to trial.
4 The trials are independent.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/03/2025 4/26
The Binomial Experiment
Consider the binomial experiment of an insurance agent calling 10
customers.
We define a success if the agent closes the sale of insurance with a
customer, and a failure if he does not.
From past data/experience, we know that the agent closes on an
insurance in 10% of the calls,psuccess = 0.10.
The stationarity assumption requires that the probability of a success
(selling the insurance) remains the same over each trial(call).
The independence of the trials regards the decision of each customer of
whether to purchase or not the insurance.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/03/2025 5/26
The Binomial Experiment
In a binomial experiment, our interest is in the numberx of successes
occurring in then trials.
Because the number of successes is finite,x is adiscrete random
variable.
The probability distribution associated with this random variable is
called the binomial probability distribution,
And it is described by thebinomial probability function.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/03/2025 6/26
The Binomial Experiment - Martin Clothing Store
On the basis of past experience, the store manager at the Martin
Clothing store estimates the probability that any one customer will
make a purchase to be 0.30.
What is the probability that two of the next three customers will
make a purchase?
The tree diagram in the next slide shows that the experiment has
eight possible outcomes.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/03/2025 7/26
The Binomial Experiment - Martin Clothing Store
The requirements for a binomial experiment are met:
Three identical trials, one for each customer entering the store
Two outcomes: S = purchase, F = no purchase
Purchase: p = 0.30; no purchase: 1−p = 0.70
Each purchase decision is independent
Figure 1: 5.5 Martin Clothing Store Problem
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/03/2025 8/26
The Binomial Probability Function
The binomial probability function determines the probability of
observing x successes in n trials in a binomial experiment.
f (x ) =
(
n
x
)
px (1−p)n−x
Where:
x = the number of successes
p = the probability of a success in one trial
n = the number of trials
f (x ) = the probability ofx successes in n trials
(n
x
)
= n!
x !(n−x )! = the number of experimental outcomes providingx
successes in n trials
px (1−p)n−x provides the probability of a particular sequence of trial
outcomes with x successes in n trials

Interval Estimation Practice
BAT2301 - Ian Liu
2025-04-09
Question 1
A survey conducted by the American Automobile Association showed that a family of four spends an average
of $215.60 per day while on vacation. Suppose a sample of 64 families of four vacationing in Corpus Christi
TX resulted in a sample mean of $252.45 per day and a sample standard deviation of $74.50.
a. Develop a 95% confidence interval estimate of the mean amount spent per day by a family of four
visiting Corpus Christi TX.
b. Based on the confidence interval from part (a), does it appear that the population mean amount
spent per day by families visiting Corpus Christi TX differs from the mean reported by the American
Automobile Association? Explain.
1
Question 2
According to Franchise Business Review, over 50% of all food franchises earn a profit of less than $50,000 a
year. In a sample of 142 casual dining restaurants, 81 earned a profit of less than $50,000 last year.
a. What is the point estimate of the proportion of casual dining restaurants that earned a profit of less
than $50,000 last year?
b. Determine the margin of error and provide a 95% confidence interval for the proportion of casual dining
restaurants that earned a profit of less than $50,000 last year.
c. How large a sample is needed if the desired margin of error is 0.03?
2

## Course Overview

**Course Description**

Welcome to BAT/ECON 2301, an introductory course on statistics and probability tailored for applications in business and economics. In this course, you will delve into the application of various statistical and probabilistic techniques within the realms of business and economics. Topics include data descriptions and their statistical significance, probability and calculations, counting rules, distributions, sampling, hypothesis testing, inferences, simple linear regression, and most importantly, why and how do we want to use these tools to solve real-world questions. We will ensure a comprehensive understanding and implementation of these techniques. The Python programming language will be introduced and self-taught via [datacamp.com](https://datacamp.com), which is an interactive learning platform specializing in coding and programming skills, tailored for both beginners and experienced professionals seeking to enhance their expertise in data science and analytics.

Identify and use the Binomial probability functionto compute

probability of a specified number of occurrences over a given interval.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/26/2025 3/28
Introduction
In this chapter, we extend the study of probability by introducing the
concepts of random variables and probability distributions.
Random variables and probability distributions are models for
populations of data.
The values of random variables represent the values of the data
The probability distribution provides either the probability of each data
value or a rule for computing the probability of each data value or a set
of data values
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/26/2025 4/28
Discrete vs. Continuous Data: What’s the
Difference?
Definition: discrete datainvolves distinct, countable values. It can
only take specific numbers (whole numbers) with no in-betweens!
Examples:
Number of pets (You can have 1, 2, or 3 pets, but not 2.5 pets)
Number of cars (You can have 1, 2, and 3 cars, but not 4.5 cars)
Number of ex’s
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/26/2025 5/28
Discrete vs. Continuous Data: What’s the
Difference?
Definition: continuous data can take any value within a range,
including fractions and decimals.
Examples:
Gas prices ($6.55/Gallon in California and $2.47/Gallon in Texas)
Time to finish 5K run (Your race time could be 15 minutes , or 15.66
minutes)
Temperature of this classroom (It could weigh 71.5F or 21.9C)
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/26/2025 6/28
Introduction
The focus of this chapter is on probability distributions fordiscrete
data, or discrete probability distributions.
We will introduce two types of discrete probability distributions.
A table with one column for the values of the random variable and a
second column for the associated probabilities.
Two types of mathematical functions(aka. probability distributions) to
handle discrete data: theBinomial and Poisson.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/26/2025 7/28
What Is A Discrete Value and Are Random
Variables?
A random variableis a numerical description of the outcome of an
experiment.
A discrete random variablemay assume
Either a finite number of values or an infinite sequence of values.
A continuous random variablemay assume
Any numerical value in an interval or collection of intervals.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/26/2025 8/28
Discrete Random Variable with a Finite Number of
Values

∑

Probability Distribution Given by a Formula
In addition to tables and graphs, a formula that gives the probability
function, f (x ), for every value ofx is often used to describe the
probability distributions.
Typical discrete probability distributions specified by formulas are the
discrete-uniform, binomial, Poisson, and hypergeometric distributions.
The discrete uniform probability distribution is the simplest example
of a discrete probability distribution given by a formula.

f (x ) = 1
n
Where: n = the number of values the random variable may assume

6

Expected Value
The expected value, or mean, of a random variable is a measure of its
central location.
The expected value of a discrete random variable is calculated as a
weighted average of the values the random variable may assume. The
weights are the probabilities.
E (x ) =
∑
xf (x )

∑
(x−µ)2f (x )

Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 09/24/2024 4/29
Introduction
In this chapter, we extend the study of probability by introducing the
concepts of random variables and probability distributions.
Random variables and probability distributions are models for
populations of data.
The values of random variables represent the values of the data
The probability distribution provides either the probability of each data
value or a rule for computing the probability of each data value or a set
of data values
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 09/24/2024 5/29
Discrete vs. Continuous Data: What’s the
Difference?
Definition: discrete datainvolves distinct, countable values. It can
only take specific numbers (whole numbers) with no in-betweens!
Examples:
Number of pets (You can have 1, 2, or 3 pets, but not 2.5 pets)
Number of cars (You can have 1, 2, and 3 cars, but not 4.5 cars)
Number of ex’s
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 09/24/2024 6/29
Discrete vs. Continuous Data: What’s the
Difference?
Definition: continuous data can take any value within a range,
including fractions and decimals.
Examples:
Gas prices ($6.55/Gallon in California and $2.47/Gallon in Texas)
Time to finish 5K run (Your race time could be 15 minutes , or 15.66
minutes)
Temperature of this classroom (It could weigh 71.5F or 21.9C)
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 09/24/2024 7/29

data, or discrete probability distributions.
We will introduce two types of discrete probability distributions.
A table with one column for the values of the random variable and a
second column for the associated probabilities.
Two types of mathematical functions that represent a probability
distribution: the Binomial and Poisson

Any numerical value in an interval or collection of intervals.

of y and compare it to the expected value ofx.

What is Hypothesis Testing? And Why Do We
Need It?
We have to make guesses/predictions all the time with very limited
information
Some are right and some are wrong
2 types of wrong answers
Set up theSTATISTICALboundaries for the guess
If your guess is within the boundaries, it’s anacceptable(correct)
guess
If your guess if out of the boundaries, it’s a false/wrong guess
Hypothesis testing is the mathematical representation of those
boundaries
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 4/45
Hypothesis Testing
Hypothesis testing can be used to determine whether a statement
about the value of a population parameter
Should or should not be rejected
Believed to be true or false
The null hypothesis, denoted byH0 , is atentative assumption about
a population parameter
The alternative hypothesis, denoted byHa or H1, is the opposite of
what is stated in the null hypothesis
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 5/45
To-Do’s for Hypothesis Testing
Step 1: grab some sample data from a population
Step 2: use some data from a sample to test the twocompeting
statements
Indicated by H0 and Ha
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 6/45
Developing Null and Alternative Hypotheses
It could be tricky to understand the context of the situation and
structure the hypotheses appropriately so that the test conclusion
provides the desired information.
All hypothesis testing applications involvecollecting a sampleand
using the sample results to provide evidence for drawing a conclusion.
Good questions to consider when formulating the null and alternative
hypotheses are:
What is the purpose of collecting the sample?
What conclusions are we hoping to make?
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 7/45
Developing Null and Alternative Hypotheses -
Example
When an attempt is being made to gather evidence in support of a
hypothesis, it is often best to begin with the alternative hypothesis
and make it the conclusion that the researcher hopes to support.
The conclusion that the research hypothesis is true is made if the
sample data provide sufficient evidence to show that the null
hypothesis can be rejected.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 8/45
An Example
Ford Motors is developing a new fuel injection that is believed to
provide an MPG of more than 24 MPG for their F-150’s.
We set the population mean miles per gallonHa :µ>24 as the
alternative hypothesis
And set the null hypothesis asH0 :µ≤24
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 9/45
Developing Null and Alternative Hypotheses
We might begin with a belief or assumption that a statement about
the value of a population parameter is true.
We then use a hypothesis test to challenge the assumption and
determine if there is statistical evidence to conclude that the
assumption is not true
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 10/45

The label on an Ozarka water bottle states that it contains 16.9 fluid

We assume the label correct
We assume the population mean filling weight,µ, is 16.9 fluid ounces,
We set it as the null hypothesis, orH0 :µ= 16.9
Then, we challenge the assumption in the alternative hypothesis
Ha :µ̸= 16.9
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 11/45
Summary of Forms for Null and Alternative
Hypotheses
Rule 1: The equality part of the hypotheses always appears in the null
hypothesis.
Rule 2: A hypothesis test about the value of a population meanµ
must take one of the followingTHREE forms, where # is the
hypothesized value of the population mean.
One-tailed (Lower-tail): H0 :µ≥# vs. Ha :µ<#
One-tailed (Upper-tail): H0 :µ≤# vs. Ha :µ>#
Two-tailed (Both Upper and Lower):H0 :µ= # vs. Ha :µ̸= #
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 12/45
Summary of Forms for Null and Alternative
Hypotheses
One-tailed (Lower-tail): H0 :µ≥# vs. Ha :µ<#
One-tailed (Upper-tail): H0 :µ≤# vs. Ha :µ>#
Two-tailed (Both Upper and Lower):H0 :µ= # vs. Ha :µ̸= #

Hypotheses
In this chapter, we show how statistical inference from sample data
and results can be used together with hypothesis testing to determine
whether a statement about thevalue of a population parameter
should or should not be rejected.
We quantify thefavor or support, if the sample results support or
favorite Ha,
Case1: We rejectH0 -> We supportH1
Case2: We do not rejectH0 -> We cannot supportH1
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 14/45
We Are Humans, We Make Mistakes/Wrong
Guesses.
Because hypothesis tests are based on sample data, we must allow for
the possibility of errors.
There are two types of error that can be made in hypothesis tests:
Type I and Type II errors.
Figure 1: Error Types
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 15/45
Type I and Type II Errors
Figure 2: Error Types
Figure 3: Correct Predictions and
Error Types
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 16/45
Type I and Type II Errors
Type I Error
A Type I error is rejectingH0 when it is true.
The probability of making a Type I error when the null hypothesis is
true as an equality is called the level of significance.
Applications of hypothesis testing that only control for the Type I error
are often called significance tests.
Type II Error
A Type II error is acceptingH0 when it is false.
Statisticians themselves avoid the risk of making a Type II error by
using “do not rejectH0” rather than “acceptH0”. - SNEAKY!!
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 17/45
Example of Type I and Type II Errors
Recall the hypothesis testing example about the new fuel injection
unit. Imagine you’ve developed a new fuel system for cars, and you
believe it makes cars more fuel-efficient than the current standard.
You decide to test if this new system really improves the car’s miles
per gallon (MPG) rating compared to the usual 24 MPG benchmark.
H0 :µ≤24
Ha :µ>24 (1)
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 18/45
Example of Type I and Type II Errors
Type I Error: This is like sounding a false alarm.
It’s when youconclude that your new fuel system does indeed improve
the car’s MPG (makes it better than 24 MPG), but in reality, it doesn’t
— It’s just the same as the old system. So, youmistakenly think
you’ve made an improvement when you haven’t
Type II Error: This is like missing something important.
It’s when you decide that your new fuel system doesn’t make any
difference (so, MPG is 24 or less)
But in fact, it actually does improve the MPG. So, you’ve overlooked a
real improvement, thinking your system didn’t make the car more
fuel-efficient when it really did.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 19/45
Example of Type I and Type II Errors
In summary, a Type I error is thinking you’ve achieved something
special when you haven’t, and a Type II error is failing to recognize an
actual achievement, thinking nothing has changed.
Type I Error: Thinking your new system works (improves MPG) when
it doesn’t.
Type II Error: Not realizing your new system works (actually improves
MPG) when it does.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 20/45
One-Tailed Test About a Population Mean
One-tailed tests about a population mean take one of the following
two forms.
Lower/Left-Tailed Test
H0 :µ≥#
Ha :µ<# (2)
One-tailed tests about a population mean take one of the following
two forms.
Upper/Right-Tailed Test
H0 :µ≤#
Ha :µ># (3)
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 21/45
One-Tailed Test About a Population Mean -
Example
Consider the example of the Federal Trade Commission (FTC) testing
a Hilltop Coffee’s claim that its large can contains at least 3 pounds
of coffee.
The FTC assumes Hilltop Coffee’s claim to be correct (µ≥3 pounds)
and sets the alternative hypothesis to challenge the claim, using the
lower tail test,µ<3 pounds. Thus, we write:
H0 :µ≥3
Ha :µ<3 (4)
The FTC is willing to take a 1% risk of making an error on its testing
of Hilltop Coffee’s claim.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 22/45

We can write the sampling distribution of¯x as normally distributed,
centered around the hypothesized mean,µ=µ0 = 3, and with

Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 24/45
p-Value Approach to a Lower Tail Test About a
Population Mean: σ Known
A p-value is a probability, computed using the test statistic, that
measures the support provided by the sample for the null hypothesis.
Suppose the sample ofn = 36 coffee cans revealed¯x = 2.92 pounds.
Then, the test statistic is
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 25/45
p-Value Approach to a Lower Tail Test About a
Population Mean: σ Known
For a lower tail test, the p-value is the probability thatz is less than
or equal to−2.67.
p−value = P(z≤−2.67) = 0.0038
This p-value indicates a small probability of obtaining a sample mean
of ¯x = 2.92 or smaller when sampling from a population withµ= 3.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 26/45
Rejection Rule Option 1: Using p-Value
In the previous slide, we concluded that the p-value of 0.0038 does
not provide much support for the null hypothesis,but is it small
enough to cause us to rejectH0?
The answerdepends upon the level of significanceαfor the test:
If a p-value is less than or equal to the level of significanceα, the value
of the test statistic is in the rejection region.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 27/45
Rejection Rule Option 1: Using p-Value
Because the FTC stated to be willing to take a 1% risk of making a
Type I error, we set the significance levelα= 0.01.
We write the rejection rule using the p-value as:
Reject H0 if p-value≤α
With the p-value = 0.0038 being less than or equal toα= 0.01, we
reject H0.
Therefore, the FTC finds sufficient statistical evidence to conclude at
the 0.01 level of significance that the filling weight of Hilltop Coffee
cans is less than 3 pounds.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 28/45
Rejection Rule Option 2: Using Critical Value
We can use the standard normal probability distribution to find thez
value with an area ofαin the lower (or upper) tail of the distribution.
The value of the test statistic that establishes the boundary of the
rejection region is called the critical value for the test.
The rejection rule for a one-tailed test using the critical value is:
Lower Tail: RejectH0 if z≤zα
Upper Tail: RejectH0 if z≥zα
zαis such thatP(z≤zα) =α.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 29/45
Rejection Rule Option 2: Using Critical Value
In the Hilltop Coffee case, whenα= 0.01, zα=−2.33.
The rejection rule for a lower tail test becomes
Reject H0 if z≤−2.33, and becausez =−2.67, we rejectH0.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 30/45
Two-Tailed Test About a Population Mean
A two-tailed test about a population mean takes the following form.
H0 :µ=µ0
Ha :µ̸=µ0
(5)
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 31/45
Two-Tailed Test About a Population Mean -
Example
Consider MaxFlight’s need of testing its manufacturing process to
ensure it produces golf balls with a mean driving distance of 295 yards.
If the golf balls drive less than 295 yards, MaxFlight will eventually lose
sales, and if the golf balls’ driving distance exceeds 295 yards, they may
be rejected by the USGA.
Thus, we assume that the mean driving distance is as specified by
MaxFlight, and challenge it in the alternative hypothesis, by writing:
H0 :µ= 295
Ha :µ̸= 295 (6)
The quality control team selectedα= 0.05 as the level of significance
for the test.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 32/45
Two-Tailed Test About a Population Mean -
Example
Suppose a sample ofn = 50 golf balls is collected, and the sample
mean ¯x = 297.6 is computed as an estimate of the population mean
µ.
Data from previous tests conducted when the process was known to
be in adjustment show that the population standard deviation can be
assumed known with a value ofσ= 12 (This isσknown case).
The sampling distribution of¯x as normally distributed, centered
around the hypothesized mean,µ=µ0 = 295, and with standard
error,σ¯x = σ√n = 1.7
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 33/45
Two-Tailed Test About a Population Mean -
Example
We write the test statistic of a hypothesis test about a population
mean: σknown, using the standard normal random variable,z, as:
z = ¯x−µ0
σ¯x
= 297.6−295
1.7 = 1.53
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 34/45
Two-Tailed Test About a Population Mean -
Example
The p-value corresponds to the probability of finding a value for z
that is at least as unlikely asz = 1.53.
For a two-tailed test of a symmetric sampling distribution, values of
z≥1.53 are just as unlikely as values ofz≤−1.53. Thus, we can
write the p-value as:
p-value = p(z≥1.53) + p(z≤−1.53)
Because the normal distribution is symmetric, we can calculate the
p-value as
p-value = p(z≥1.53) + p(z≤−1.53) = 0.0630 +0.0630 = 0.126
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 35/45
Two-Tailed Test About a Population Mean -
Example
Next, we apply the rejection rule:
Because p-value= 0.126> 0.05
(The quality control team selectedα= 0.05 as the level of significance
for the test.)
We cannot rejectH0.
Thus, no action will be taken to adjust the MaxFlight’s manufacturing
process.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 36/45
Rejection Rule Using Critical Value for a Two-Tailed
Test
The rejection rule for a two-tailed test using the critical value is:
Reject H0 if z≤−zα/2 or z≥zα/2
In the golf ball case, we haveα= 0.05.
Thus, α/2 = 0.025, andzα/2 = z0.025 = 1.96
The rejection rule for this two-tail test becomes
Reject H0 if z≤−1.96 or z≥1.96
Because the value of the test statistic for the golf ball study is
z = 1.53, the statistical evidence willNOT permit us to reject the null
hypothesis at the 0.05 level of significance.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 37/45

Point Estimation
Point estimation is a form of statistical inference
In point estimation we use the data from the sample to compute a
value of a sample statistic that serves as an estimate of a population
parameter
We refer to¯x as the point estimator of the population meanµ
s is the point estimator of the population standard deviationσ
¯p is the point estimator of the population proportionp

Central Limit Theorem(CLT)
Z Distribution/Score helps turn any non-standard normal distributions
into standard normal distribution
CLT helps turn any distributions into standard normal distribution!!!
Figure 8: Why Is CLT So Useful
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/22/2024 20/29
Central Limit Theorem(CLT)
As the sample size gets large enough, the sampling distribution
becomes almost normal regardless of shape of population
This magic trick is true all the time!
Why is CLT useful?
It allows the use of normal distribution assumptions for sample means,
regardless of the original data’s distribution. This simplification enables
easier statistical analysis, hypothesis testing, and confidence interval
construction
It’s widely applicable across various fields for making informed decisions
based on sample data

Central Limit Theorem(CLT) Example
You are managing a company that sells handmade candles
Where the average production cost per candle is $8(µ= 8) with a
variability in cost (standard deviation) of $3(σ= 3). The company
decides to take a random sample of 36 candles(n = 36) to analyze
production expenses.
The question becomes: What is the probability that the average
production cost for this sample of candles is between $7.8 and $8.2?
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/22/2024 24/29
Central Limit Theorem(CLT)
Central Limit Theorem(CLT) is like an universal key that can unlock
any different types of distributions and predict the probability with
the help of normal distribution
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/22/2024 25/29
Central Limit Theorem(CLT)
By leveraging the CLT, businesses can apply statistical methods
confidently, enhancing decision-making, improving products and
services, and ultimately achieving strategic goals with greater
efficiency.
Quality Control: In manufacturing, CLT helps assess the quality of
products. By sampling a few products from a production line,
companies can predict the overall quality and make adjustments
without inspecting every item.
Customer Satisfaction: Businesses can survey a sample of customers
about their satisfaction levels. CLT assures that even if individual
opinions are varied, the average satisfaction score from multiple
samples will form a normal distribution, helping to gauge overall
customer sentiment accurately.
Financial Forecasting: For investment analysis, CLT helps in predicting
the average returns of a portfolio. By understanding the distribution of
average returns, businesses can assess risk and make informed
investment decisions.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/22/2024 26/29

three months of 2020 was $279.19. Assume the standard deviation for this
population of fares is known to be $80. Suppose a random sample of 60
flights departing from San Antonio International Airport during the first
three months of 2022 is taken.
a. If the mean and standard deviation of the population of airfares for
flights departing from the airport didn’t changed between the first
three months of 2021 and the first three months of 2022, what is the
probability the sample mean will be within $20 of the population
mean cost per flight?

Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/08/2024 3 / 25
Introduction
In this chapter, we study continuous random variables and discuss
three continuous probability distributions:
The continuous uniform,
The exponential,
The normal.
Because of its wide applicability and its extensive use in statistical
inference in business, economics and engineering, the normal
distribution is just the big boss.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/08/2024 4 / 25
Introduction
Probabilities are computed differently for discrete and continuous
random variables.
With discrete random variables, the probability functionf (x ) provides
the probability that the random variable assumes a particular value.
With continuous random variables,
The probability that x assumes a particular value is zero, and a value in
a given interval is the corresponding area under the graph of f (x );
f (x ) is called the Probability Density Function(PDF).
Continuous probability distribution: A probability distribution in
which the continuous random variablex can take on any value.
Because there are infinite values that X could assume, the probability
of x taking on any one specific value is zero.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/08/2024 5 / 25
Why Called PDF?
What’s the area of a square?
What’s the area of a rectangle?
What’s the area of a circle?
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/08/2024 6 / 25
Why Called PDF?
What’s the area of the following shape?
Figure 1: A Weird Continuous Distribution
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/08/2024 7 / 25
Continuous Uniform Distribution
A random variable is described by a uniform probability distribution
whenever the probability is proportional to the interval’s length.
In English, The probability density function for the continuous
uniform distribution is constant across the entire range.
The uniform probability density function is:
f (x ) =
{ 1
b−a for a≤x≤b
0 elsewhere (1)
Where
a = smallest value the variablex can assume
b = largest value the variablex can assume
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/08/2024 8 / 25
Continuous Uniform Distribution Examples
New Product Pricing : When a company launches a new product
and has limited historical pricing data, it might assume that the
acceptable price range to consumers is uniformly distributed. For
example, if the company believes consumers will pay between $50 and
$100 for the product, every price within this range is considered
equally likely until further market research is conducted.
Demand Forecasting During a Limited-Time Event : For events
with little historical data, such as a newly inaugurated annual sale or
a pop-up shop, the expected number of visitors or customers per day
could be modeled as uniformly distributed within a reasonable range,
assuming each day of the event is equally promoted and accessible.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/08/2024 9 / 25
Uniform Distribution Examples
Task Completion Time: If a business process is new or lacks enough
historical data to suggest a more specific distribution for the time it
takes to complete a task, managers might initially assume a uniform
distribution within a plausible time frame. For example, if they
estimate a task can take between 2 to 4 hours, they might assume
any specific duration within this range is equally likely.
Scheduling: For a facility that operates tours or appointments and
has a maximum capacity limit per time slot, but no data to suggest
peak times, it could assume the arrival of guests or clients is
uniformly distributed
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/08/2024 10 / 25

Area as a Measure of Probability
The flight time of an airplane traveling from Chicago to New York
can be described by a continuous random variable
The historic data shows that all non-delayed flights take 120 to 140
minutes.
The flight time is uniformly distributed between 120 and 140 minutes.
That is, the probability of the flight time being within any 1-minute
interval between 120 and 140 minutes is equal for each minute.
Thus, the random variablex is said to have the following uniform
probability distribution.
f (x ) =
{ 1
140−120 for 120≤x≤140
0 elsewhere (2)

Exponential Probability Distribution
The exponential probability distribution is used for random variables
that describe the length of the interval between occurrences, such as
the time between arrivals at a hospital emergency room.
The exponential probability density function is
f (x ) = 1
µe− x
µ for x≥0
- Whereµis the expected value or mean.
Properties: under the exponential distribution, the mean(µ) and
standard deviation(σ) are the same. Variance isσ2.

P(X ) = e− x

Distribution

distribution, do NOT useF (x ) or f (x ), use P(x ) instead.
To compute exponential probabilities, we use this formula for the
cumulative probability of obtaining a value for the exponential random
variable that is less than or equal to some specific value denoted byx.
P(X ) = 1−e− x

Normal Probability Distribution
The normal/Gaussian probability distribution is the most common
probability continuous distribution.
It’s used in a wide variety of practical applications.
It is widely applied to statistical inference
Something we will be using all the time from now on
The normal distribution assumes the classic bell-shaped curve and
provides a description of the likely results obtained through sampling.
We will talk about sampling next week.
Derived and proved by Abraham de Moivre, a French mathematician.
Work published on The Doctrine of Chances in 1733.
Same year the Molasses Act was imposed
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/15/2024 3 / 33
Normal Probability Density Function
The normal probability density function is described by two
parameters: the meanµand the standard deviationσ.
f (x ) = 1
σ
√
2πe− 1
2 ( x −µ
σ )2
(1)
Where
π= 3.14159
e = 2.71828 - Euler’s Nmebr
Figure 1: Normal Distribution
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/15/2024 4 / 33
The Mean of the Normal Distribution
We make the following observations about the meanµof the normal
distribution:
1 The meanµis the highest point on the normal curve.
2 The meanµis also the median and mode of the distribution.
3 The mean of the distribution can be any numerical value: negative,
zero, or positive.
4 The normal distribution or the bell-shaped curve is symmetric.
5 The area under the curve to the left of the mean and the area under
the curve to the right of the mean are both 0.50.
6 The tails of the normal curve extend to infinity in both directions and
theoretically never touch the horizontal axis.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/15/2024 5 / 33

The figure shows three normal distributions with the sameσand µ=
-10, 0 , and 20.

The Spread of the Normal Distribution
We make the following observations about the standard deviationσ
of the normal distribution:
1 The standard deviation determines how flat and wide the normal curve
is.
2 Larger values ofσresult in wider, flatter curves, showing more
variability in the data.
The figure shows two normal distributions with the same mean butσ
= 5 andσ=10.
3 The mean determines the location of the curve, SD determines the
shape of the curve.

Standard Normal Probability Distribution
We need a tool to help us unify or convert these non-standard normal
distributions to a standard normal distribution.
A random variable having a normal distribution witha mean of 0
and a standard deviation of 1 is said to have a standard normal
probability distribution.
The standard normal distribution has the same general appearance as
other normal distributions, but with the special properties of having
µ= 0 andσ= 1
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/15/2024 11 / 33
Standard Normal Probability Distribution
Because ofµ= 0 andσ= 1, the formula for the standard normal
probability density function is a simpler version of the equation shown
earlier for the general normal probability distribution:
f (x ) = 1√
2πe− x 2
2 (2)

Standard Normal Probability Distribution
As with other continuous random variables, probability calculations
with a normal distribution are made by computing the area under the
graph of the normal curve over a given interval.
We will now show how to use the Z-Table to calculate the following
cumulative probabilities associated with a standard normal probability
distribution
The probability that the standard normal random variablez will be less
than or equal to a given valueZ: P(z≤Z1)
The probability thatz will be between two given values,
Z1 and Z2 : P(Z1≤z≤Z2)
The probability thatz will be greater than or equal to a given value
P(z≥Z2)
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/15/2024 15 / 33
Standard Normal Probability Distribution
The probability that the standard normal random variablez will be
less than or equal to a given valueZ: P(z≤Z1)
The probability thatz will be between two given values,
Z1 and Z2 : P(Z1≤z≤Z2)
The probability thatz will be greater than or equal to a given value
P(z≥Z2)
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/15/2024 16 / 33
Cumulative Probability for P(z≤1.00)
The cumulative probability thatz is less than or equal to 1.00, that
is, P(z≤1.00), is the area under the standard normal curve to the
left of z = 1.00
To find the cumulative probability corresponding toz≤1.00 in the
Z-Table,
We identify the value located at the intersection of the row labeled 1.0
and the column labeled 0.00.
We find 1.0 in the left column of the table
We find 0.00 in the top row of the table.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/15/2024 17 / 33
Cumulative Probability for P(z≤1.00)
By looking in the body of the table, we find that the 1.0 row and the
0.00 column intersect at the value of 0.8413. Thus
P(z≤1.00) = 0.8413

Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/15/2024 19 / 33
Cumulative Probability for P(−0.50≤z≤1.25)
Three steps are required to compute this probability using the Z-Table
1 We calculate P(z≤−0.50) as the intersection of the –0.5 row and the
0.00 column:
P(z≤−0.50) = 0.3085
2 We calculate P(Z≤1.25) as the intersection of the 1.2 row and the
0.05 column:
P(z≤1.25) = 0.8944
3 We calculate the difference of the cumulative probabilities to the left of
z = 1.25 and z =−0.50 that we calculated in steps 1 and 2, so that:
P(−0.50≤z≤1.25) = P(z≤1.25)−P(z≤−0.50) = 0.5859
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/15/2024 20 / 33
Cumulative Probability for P(z≥1.58)
The cumulative probability thatz is greater than or equal to 1.58,
that is, P(z≥1.58), is the area under the standard normal curve to
the right ofz = 1.58.
To find the probability to the right ofz = 1.58, we need to first find
the probability corresponding to the left ofz = 1.58, and then
calculate its complement.
To calculate the area to the left ofz = 1.58, identify the value
located at the intersection of the row labeled 1.5 and the column
labeled 0.08. Thus, P(z≤1.58) = 0.9429
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/15/2024 21 / 33
Cumulative Probability for P(z≥1.58)
However, because the total area under the normal curve is 1, we have
P(z≥1.58) = 1−0.9429 = 0.0571
Figure 10: P(z≥1.58)
 Figure 11: Find P(z≥1.58)
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/15/2024 22 / 33
Find Z Such That P(z≥z∗) = 0.10
We want to find az ∗ value such that the probability of obtaining a
larger z value is 0.10. That is
P(z≥z ∗) = 0.10
In this inverse problem, we need to find thez value that corresponds
to a given probability.
However, the standard normal probability table gives the area under
the curve to the left of a particularz value.
Thus, we use again the complement rule to find az ∗ value such that
P(z≤z ∗) = 1−P(z≥z ∗) = 1−0.10 = 0.90

Grear Tire Company has developed a new steel-belted radial tire to be

reasonable assumption.
Thus, we define a normal random variable asx = number of miles the
tire will last

Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/15/2024 26 / 33
Probability Tire Mileage Exceeds 40,000 Miles
As depicted by the darker area in the figure, we need to find
P(x≥40,000).
We can convert the normal random variablex to the standard normal
random variable z, as
z = x−µ
σ = 40,000−36,500
5,000 = 3,500
5,000 = 0.70
The probability thatz is greater than or equal to 0.70 is the
complement to the probability thatz is less than or equal to 0.70.
Thus,
P(x≥40,000) = P(x≥0.70) = 1−P(z≤0.70) = 1−0.7580 = 0.2420
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/15/2024 27 / 33
Probability Tire Mileage Exceeds 40,000 Miles
We can conclude that about 24.2% of the tires will exceed 40,000 in
mileage.

σ

Continuous Probability Distribution Practice
BAT2301 - Ian Liu
2025-03-24
Q1
Most graduate schools of business require applicants for admission to take the Graduate Management
Admission Council’s GMAT examination. Scores on the GMAT are roughly normally distributed with a
mean of 527 and a standard deviation of 112.
a. What is the probability of an individual scoring above 500 on the GMAT?
b. How high must an individual score on the GMAT in order to score in the highest 5%?
Q2
The average number of acres burned by forest and range fires in a large New Mexico county is 4,300 acres per
year, with a standard deviation of 750 acres. The distribution of the number of acres burned is normal.
a. What is the probability that between 2,500 and 4,200 acres will be burned in any given year?
b. What number of burnt acres corresponds to the 38th percentile?
1
Q3
The Texas DOT wants to study how much money daily commuters spend on gas per gallon. The historic
data revealed that the spending distribution is approximately normally distributed with a mean of $4.11 and
a standard deviation of $1.37.
a. What percentage of daily commuters will spend less than $3.00 per gallon on gas?
b. What spending amount corresponds to the top 87th percentile?
Q4
The United States Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) recommends that adults sleep 7 to
9 hours per night (CDC.gov). However, many adults in the United States sleep less than seven hours per
night. Suppose that the amount of sleep for an adult in the United States follows a normal distribution with
a mean of 8.0 hours and standard deviation of 1.7 hours.
a. What is the probability that an adult in the United States sleeps less than 7.0 hours?
b. What is the probability that an adult in the United States sleeps between 7.0 and 9.0 hours?
c. How many hours of sleep would an adult in the United States get if they are in the 10th percentile of
this distribution?
2
Q5
Supermarket chain H-E-B has used computer simulation and information technology to reduce the average
waiting time for customers at 2300 stores. Using a new system called QueVision, which allows H-E-B to better
predict when shoppers will be checking out, the company was able to decrease average customer waiting time
to just 26 seconds.
a. Assume that H-E-B waiting times are exponentially distributed. Show the probability density function
of waiting time at H-E-B.
b. What is the probability that a customer will have to wait between 15 and 30 seconds?
c. What is the probability that a customer will have to wait more than 2 minutes?
Q6
According to a survey, college students in the U.S. spend 8.35 hours daily on phones on average. Use a normal
probability distribution with a standard deviation of 2.5 hours to answer the following questions about daily
screen time.
a. What is the probability that a student at Trinity spends between 5 and 10 hours on their phone a day?
b. How many hours of screen time must a student have in order to be in the top 3% of all students in
terms of screen time?
c. (Team Presentation for 10/15) What is the probability that a student spends more than 3 hours on the
phone a day?
3
Q7
The electric vehicle manufacturing company Tesla estimates that a driver who commutes 50 miles per day in
a Model S will require a nightly charge time of around 1 hour and 45 minutes (105 minutes) to recharge the
vehicle’s battery (Tesla company website). Assume that the actual recharging time required is uniformly
distributed between 90 and 120 minutes.
a. Give a mathematical expression for the probability density function of battery recharging time for this
scenario.
b. What is the probability that the recharge time will be less than 110 minutes?
c. What is the probability that the recharge time required is at least 100 minutes?
d. What is the probability that the recharge time required is between 95 and 110 minutes?
Continuous Probability Formulas

Test Hypotheses Test Statistic Rejection Rule
Lower Tail Test H0 :µ≥µ0
Ha :µ<µ0 z = ¯x−µ0
σ/√n P-value: Reject H0 if p≤α
Critical Value: RejectH0 if z≤−zα
Upper Tail Test H0 :µ≤µ0
Ha :µ>µ0 z = ¯x−µ0
σ/√n P-value: Reject H0 if p≤α
Critical Value: RejectH0 if z≥zα
Two-Tailed Test H0 :µ=µ0
Ha :µ̸=µ0 z = ¯x−µ0
σ/√n P-value: Reject H0 if p≤α
Critical Value: RejectH0 if|z|≥zα/2

An Example - Engineering Approach
The t-test is used to determine if there is a significant difference
between the means of two groups. The t-value is calculated using the
formula:
t =
¯X1 − ¯X2√
s 2
n1
+ s 2
n2
Where ¯X1 and ¯X2 are the sample means,s 2 is the pooled sample variance,
and n1 and n2 are the sample sizes.
The p-value is then calculated based on this t-value and the degrees of
freedom of the test. It represents the probability of observing a test
statistic as extreme as, or more extreme than, the observed value under
the null hypothesis.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT/ECON 2301 - Statistics for Business 01/15/2025 8 / 26
An Example - My Approach
Let me ask you something:
Do you have to be a mechanical engineer to be able to drive a car?
Do you have to be a electrical/computer engineer to be able to use a
computer?
Statistics is a practical implication/subject, just like a tool
The fundamental statistics is not a science subject like physics or
mathematics
Some statisticians/mathematicians may disagree with me on this
statement
Advanced statistics is a science.
Treat it like a tool, you just need to know how to use it
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT/ECON 2301 - Statistics for Business 01/15/2025 9 / 26
An Example - My Approach
Figure 1: A Model is Just a Recipe
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT/ECON 2301 - Statistics for Business 01/15/2025 10 / 26
An Example - My Approach
Step 1: Collect Data & Calculate the Averages
You collect the monthly returns for each stock over the past 12 months.
Valero: Monthly returns varied from -2% to +3%.
Apple: Monthly returns varied from -1% to +4%.
You calculate the average monthly return for each stock. Let’s say:
Valero had an average monthly return of 0.5%.
Apple had an average monthly return of 1.0%.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT/ECON 2301 - Statistics for Business 01/15/2025 11 / 26
An Example - My Approach
Step 2: Use the t-test
The t-test is a tool that will help you determine if the observed
difference in average returns (0.5% in this case) is statistically significant
or could have occurred by chance.
Why t-test: you apply the t-test to compare these two sets of monthly
returns.
The t-test will consider:
The difference between the two averages (0.5% for Stock A and 1.0%
for Stock B).
The number of data points (12 months for each stock).
The variability in monthly returns for each stock.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT/ECON 2301 - Statistics for Business 01/15/2025 12 / 26
An Example - My Approach
Step 3: Analyze the Result
The t-test will provide a t-value and a p-value:
The t-value measures the size of the difference relative to the variation
in your sample data.
The p-value indicates the probability of seeing a difference as large as (or
larger than) what you observed
If the p-value is low (e.g., less than 0.05), it suggests that the difference
in average returns is statistically significant. In other words, it’s unlikely
that this difference is due to random chance, and you might conclude
that Stock B genuinely had better performance over the past year.
If the p-value is high (e.g., more than 0.05) would indicate that the
difference in average returns might just be due to random fluctuations,
and you can’t confidently assert that one stock outperformed the other.

Q1: Americans Using Facebook and LinkedIn. A 2018 Pew Research Center survey (Pew Research website) examined the use of social media platforms in the United States. The survey found that there is a 0.68 probability that a randomly selected American will use Facebook and a 0.25 probability that a randomly selected American will use LinkedIn. In addition, there is a 0.22 probability that a randomly selected American will use both Facebook and LinkedIn.  a. What is the probability that a randomly selected American will use Facebook or LinkedIn?   b. What is the probability that a randomly selected American will not use either social media platform?   Q2: Morningstar Mutual Fund Ratings.  Information about mutual funds provided by Morningstar includes the type of mutual fund ( Domestic Equity, International Equity, or Fixed Income) and the Morningstar rating for the fund. The rating is expressed from 1-star ( lowest rating) to 5-star ( highest rating). Suppose a sample of 25 mutual funds provided the following counts: • Sixteen mutual funds were Domestic Equity funds. • Thirteen mutual funds were rated 3-star or less. • Seven of the Domestic Equity funds were rated 4-star. • Two of the Domestic Equity funds were rated 5-star. Assume that one of these 25 mutual funds will be randomly selected in order to learn more about the mutual fund and its investment strategy.  a. What is the probability of selecting a Domestic Equity fund?  b. What is the probability of selecting a fund with a 4-star or 5-star rating?   c. What is the probability of selecting a fund that is both a Domestic Equity fund and a fund with a 4-star or 5-star rating?   d. What is the probability of selecting a fund that is a Domestic Equity fund or a fund with a 4-star or 5-star rating?   
Q3: Living with Family.  Consider the following example survey results of 18- to 34-year- olds in the United States, in response to the question “Are you currently living with your family?”   Yes No Totals Male Female 106 141   92 161 247 253 Totals 198 302 500 a. Develop the joint probability table for these data and use it to answer the following questions.      b. What are the marginal probabilities?    c. What is the probability of living with family given you are an 18- to 34-year-old male in the United States?    d. What is the probability of living with family given you are an 18- to 34-year-old female in the United States?    e. What is the probability of an 18- to 34-year-old in the United States living with family?     f. If, in the United States, 49.4% of 18- to 34-year-olds are male, do you consider this a good representative sample? Why?

Topic 1 - Sampling and Sampling Distributions
5 Questions

Continuous Probability Distribution Practice
BAT2301 - Ian Liu
2024-10-17
Q1
Most graduate schools of business require applicants for admission to take the Graduate Management Ad-
mission Council’s GMAT examination. Scores on the GMAT are roughly normally distributed with a mean
of 527 and a standard deviation of 112.
a. What is the probability of an individual scoring above 500 on the GMAT?
b. How high must an individual score on the GMAT in order to score in the highest 5%?
Q2 (Team Presentation for 10/17)
The average number of acres burned by forest and range fires in a large New Mexico county is 4,300 acres
per year, with a standard deviation of 750 acres. The distribution of the number of acres burned is normal.
a. What is the probability that between 2,500 and 4,200 acres will be burned in any given year?
b. What number of burnt acres corresponds to the 38th percentile?
1
Q3
The Texas DOT wants to study how much money daily commuters spend on gas per gallon. The historic
data revealed that the spending distribution is approximately normally distributed with a mean of $4.11 and
a standard deviation of $1.37.
a. What percentage of daily commuters will spend less than $3.00 per gallon on gas?
b. What spending amount corresponds to the top 87th percentile?
Q4
The United States Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) recommends that adults sleep 7 to
9 hours per night (CDC.gov). However, many adults in the United States sleep less than seven hours per
night. Suppose that the amount of sleep for an adult in the United States follows a normal distribution with
a mean of 8.0 hours and standard deviation of 1.7 hours.
a. What is the probability that an adult in the United States sleeps less than 7.0 hours?
b. What is the probability that an adult in the United States sleeps between 7.0 and 9.0 hours?
c. How many hours of sleep would an adult in the United States get if they are in the 10th percentile of
this distribution?
2
Q5
Supermarket chain H-E-B has used computer simulation and information technology to reduce the average
waiting time for customers at 2300 stores. Using a new system called QueVision, which allows H-E-B to
better predict when shoppers will be checking out, the company was able to decrease average customer
waiting time to just 26 seconds.
a. Assume that H-E-B waiting times are exponentially distributed. Show the probability density function
of waiting time at H-E-B.
b. What is the probability that a customer will have to wait between 15 and 30 seconds?
c. What is the probability that a customer will have to wait more than 2 minutes?
Q6
According to a survey, college students in the U.S. spend 8.35 hours daily on phones on average. Use a
normal probability distribution with a standard deviation of 2.5 hours to answer the following questions
about daily screen time.
a. What is the probability that a student at Trinity spends between 5 and 10 hours on their phone a
day?
b. How many hours of screen time must a student have in order to be in the top 3% of all students in
terms of screen time?
c. (Team Presentation for 10/15) What is the probability that a student spends more than 3 hours on
the phone a day?
3
Q7
The electric vehicle manufacturing company Tesla estimates that a driver who commutes 50 miles per day
in a Model S will require a nightly charge time of around 1 hour and 45 minutes (105 minutes) to recharge
the vehicle’s battery (Tesla company website). Assume that the actual recharging time required is uniformly
distributed between 90 and 120 minutes.
a. Give a mathematical expression for the probability density function of battery recharging time for this
scenario.
b. What is the probability that the recharge time will be less than 110 minutes?
c. What is the probability that the recharge time required is at least 100 minutes?
d. What is the probability that the recharge time required is between 95 and 110 minutes?

BAT2301 - Statistics for Business
Lecture 9 Introduction to Discrete Probability Distributions - 2
Ian Liu
Trinity University
03/03/2025
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/03/2025 1/26
What Is Binomial Distribution? And When To Use
It?
A binomial distributionis a statistical tool to model the number of
successes in a fixed number of trials (or experiments) where each trial
has only two possible outcomes: success or failure.
Think of it as flipping a coin multiple times and counting how many
heads (successes) you get.
Use the binomial distribution when:
You want to model the number of times an event occurs (like getting
heads) in a fixed number of attempts (like flipping a coin).
You’re dealing with situations where there are only two possible
outcomes (success or failure) for each attempt.
You know the probability of success for each trial and want to find out
the likelihood of getting a specific number of successes.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/03/2025 2/26
Difference Between Uniform Distribution and
Binomial Distribution
Binomial Distribution: Used when you have a fixed number of trials,
each with two possible outcomes (success or failure). You’re
interested in the number of successes.
Example: Flipping a coin 10 times and counting how many heads
(successes) you get.
Uniform Distribution: Used when all outcomes are equally likely.
Every possible outcome has the same probability.
Example: Rolling a fair die, where each of the six faces (1, 2, 3, 4, 5,
or 6) has an equal chance of showing up.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/03/2025 3/26
The Binomial Experiment
A binomial experiment has the following four properties.
1 The experiment consists of a sequence ofn identical trials.
2 Two outcomes, success and failure, are possible in each trial.
3 The stationarity assumption: the probability of a success, denoted by
p, and the probability of a failure, denoted by 1−p, do not change
from trial to trial.
4 The trials are independent.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/03/2025 4/26
The Binomial Experiment
Consider the binomial experiment of an insurance agent calling 10
customers.
We define a success if the agent closes the sale of insurance with a
customer, and a failure if he does not.
From past data/experience, we know that the agent closes on an
insurance in 10% of the calls,psuccess = 0.10.
The stationarity assumption requires that the probability of a success
(selling the insurance) remains the same over each trial(call).
The independence of the trials regards the decision of each customer of
whether to purchase or not the insurance.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/03/2025 5/26
The Binomial Experiment
In a binomial experiment, our interest is in the numberx of successes
occurring in then trials.
Because the number of successes is finite,x is adiscrete random
variable.
The probability distribution associated with this random variable is
called the binomial probability distribution,
And it is described by thebinomial probability function.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/03/2025 6/26
The Binomial Experiment - Martin Clothing Store
On the basis of past experience, the store manager at the Martin
Clothing store estimates the probability that any one customer will
make a purchase to be 0.30.
What is the probability that two of the next three customers will
make a purchase?
The tree diagram in the next slide shows that the experiment has
eight possible outcomes.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/03/2025 7/26
The Binomial Experiment - Martin Clothing Store
The requirements for a binomial experiment are met:
Three identical trials, one for each customer entering the store
Two outcomes: S = purchase, F = no purchase
Purchase: p = 0.30; no purchase: 1−p = 0.70
Each purchase decision is independent
Figure 1: 5.5 Martin Clothing Store Problem

The Binomial Probability Function
The binomial probability function determines the probability of
observing x successes in n trials in a binomial experiment.
f (x ) =
(
n
x
)
px (1−p)n−x
Where:
x = the number of successes
p = the probability of a success in one trial
n = the number of trials
f (x ) = the probability ofx successes in n trials
(n
x
)
= n!
x !(n−x )! = the number of experimental outcomes providingx
successes in n trials
px (1−p)n−x provides the probability of a particular sequence of trial
outcomes with x successes in n trials

To use the table of binomial probabilities

of interest.
For example, to obtain the probability ofx = 3 successes in a binomial
experiment with n = 10 andp = 0.40
First, find the group forn = 10. Second
Second, locate x = 3 from the available rows.
Third, move along the row forn = 10 andx = 3 until you reach the
column corresponding top = 0.40.
The answer is 0.2150.

Properties of the Binomial Distribution
In the previous section, we provided formulas for computing the
expected value and variance of a discrete random variable.
If the random variable has a binomial distribution with a known
number of n trials and a knownp probability of success, the general
formulas for the expected value and variance are
E (x ) = µ= np And Var(x ) = σ2 = np(1−p)

Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/03/2025 16/26
Poisson Probability Distribution
A Poisson Probability Distribution describesx, the number of
occurrences over a specified interval of time or space, if the
following two properties of a Poisson experiment are respected
The probability of an occurrence is the same for any two intervals of
equal length.
The occurrence or nonoccurrence in any interval is independent of the
occurrence or nonoccurrence in any other interval.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/03/2025 17/26
Poisson Probability Distribution
The Poisson probability function is described by the following equation
f (x ) = µx e−µ
x !
Where:
x = a discrete random variable describing the number of occurrences
over an interval.
f (x ) = the probability of x occurrences in an interval
µ= expected value or mean number of occurrences in an interval
NOTE: In a Poisson probability distribution,σ2 = u (the variance is
equal to the mean.)
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/03/2025 18/26
Poisson Probability Distribution Example
An analysis of historical data shows that patients arrive at the
emergency room of a large hospital on weekend mornings at the
average rateµ=10 over a 15-minute interval.
What is the probability of exactlyx = 5 arrivals in 15 minutes on a
weekend morning?
In a Poisson experiment, we have
f (5) = µx e−µ
x ! = 105e−10
5! = 0.0378 = 3.78%
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/03/2025 19/26
Poisson Probability Distribution Example
What if we want to know the probability of exactlyx=1 arrival over a
3-minute interval?
We observe that, on average, over a 3-minute interval, we have
µ= 10
15×(3) = 2. Thus, the probability of one arrival over a 3-minute
period is
f (1) = µx e−µ
x ! = 21e−2
1! = 0.2707 = 27.07%
The probability of observing 5 arrivals over a 15-minute period is not
the same as observing 1 arrival over a 3-minute period.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/03/2025 20/26
Properties of the Poisson Probability Distribution
Because there is no stated upper limit for the number of occurrences,
the Poisson probability functionf (x ) is applicable for valuesx =
0,1,2,... without an upper limit.
In practical applications,x will eventually become large enough so
that f (x ) becomes approximately zero and the probability of any
larger values ofx negligible.
A Poisson probability distribution has the variance equal to the mean:
µ=σ2
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/03/2025 21/26
Why Are Mean and Variance the Same for Poisson?
Normally, we know that the variance represents how much the number
of events fluctuates around the mean. In other words, it shows how
much deviation we expect from the average number of occurrences.
For events that following the poisson distribution, rareness and
randomness are being modeled:
Cars speeding is a rare event in the sense that not every car speeds, but
over time, you’ll observe a consistent average.
A speeding car is a rare and random event, which is an independent
event as well.
The number of speeding cars in any given hour is independent of the
number in the previous or next hour (randomness).
That’s why we define them the same.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/03/2025 22/26
Properties of the Poisson Probability Distribution
Probabilities can be calculated using the Poisson Probability Table, if
you don’t have a TI-84 calculator.
For example, to obtain the probability ofx=5 arrivals over a
15-minute period whenµ=10 using the table, follow these two steps:
First, scroll down the leftmost column until you findx=5
Second, move along the row forx = 5 until you reach the column
corresponding toµ= 10.
The answer is 0.0378.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/03/2025 23/26
Properties of the Poisson Probability Distribution
Remember that a Poisson probability distribution has the variance
equal to the mean?
Therefore, over a 15-minute interval,
We haveµ=10, the standard deviation,σ=
√
σ2 =
√
10 = 3.16
A coefficient of variation of3.16
10 ×100% = 31.6% .
Over a 3-minute interval,
We haveµ= 2 andσ=
√
σ2 =
√
2 = 1.41
And a much larger coefficient of variation of1.41
2 ×100% = 70.7%
That’s the reason why the probabilities are also very different.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/03/2025 24/26
Let’s Work on An Example Together
In a one-year period, The State of Texas had a total of 11,232 motor

accidents per hour. Assume the number of vehicle accidents that

period.

**BAT/ECON 2301 – Statistics for Business and Economics Syllabus**

<hr>

## Course Learning Objectives

By the end of this course, students will be able to:

- Understand the basics of statistical and probabilistic terminology and concepts.
- Choose appropriate methods for data collection and analysis in different contexts.
- Perform descriptive statistics to summarize and interpret data.
- Apply probability and statistics concepts to solve problems involving uncertainty.
- Conduct hypothesis tests and make informed decisions based on statistical significance.
- Interpret and analyze correlation and regression relationships between variables.
- Utilize Python for data analysis and visualization.
- Add a couple of highlighted points (programming skills, statistical inference, data modeling, and/or programming) to your resume.
- Handle basic statistics and probability-related interview questions.
- Obtain 6 certificates in Python Programming and Python for AI issued by datacamp.com which you can put on your resume.
  
## Textbooks and Computer Programming Tools

Textbooks are optional for this class to save you $$. However, I have been heavily using the following two textbooks to prepare my lectures, homeworks, and other teaching materials. Both of the physical and electronic copies are available for purchase. So it's up to you.

|          |                          |             |
|------------------------------------|------------------|------------------|
| [Statistics for Business & Economics](https://www.cengage.com/c/new-edition/9780357715857/) | Anderson, Sweeney, Williams etc.       | Cengage, 15th Edition (Earlier Editions OKAY To Use)       |
| [Business Statistics and Analytics in Practice](https://www.mheducation.com/highered/product/business-statistics-and-analytics-in-practice-bowerman.html?pd=search&viewOption=student) | Bowerman, Drougas, Duckworth etc.       | McGrow Hill, 2025 Release Edition (Earlier Editions OKAY To Use)       |

## Quantitative Reasoning (QR) Course

- **Learning Outcomes**: Upon successful completion of a Quantitative Reasoning (QR) course, student will have demonstrated the ability to:
  - Represent and solve a problem in a framework involving mathematics, statistics, computation, or symbolic logic.
  - Communicate their results.
  - Describe the significance and limitations of the quantitative approach.

- **Course Requirements**: To be designated as fulfilling the Quantitative Reasoning (QR) requirement, a course will:
  - Enable students to represent and solve problems within a quantitative framework involving mathematics, statistics, computation, or symbolic logic.
  - Provide diverse opportunities for students to articulate both their process and communicate their results.
  - Be structured so that quantitative methods are the primary focus of the course.
  - Articulate the official Learning Outcomes (see above) in the course syllabus.
  - Include a warning to students in the syllabus that if the course is approved for both an Approach and an Interdisciplinary Cluster, completion of the course will satisfy only one of the two requirements.

- **Student Requirements**: To receive credit for successful completion of the Quantitative Reasoning (QR) re-quirement, students must engage in appropriate learning activities to enable them to demonstrate the learning outcomes.



## Digital Literacy (DL) Course

- **Learning Outcomes**: Upon successful completion of a Digital Literacy (DL) course, students will have demonstrated the ability to:
  - Manipulate digital information by programming, scripting, or executing structured sequences of software commands to solve problems or engage in artistic expression.

- **Course Requirements**: To be designated as fulfilling the Digital Literacy (DL) requirement, a course will:
  - Provide instruction both in the theory and the techniques necessary to complete a digital work using at least one appropriate technology, with the focus on de- veloping the technical facility (Students must learn “how” to complete a digital work using at least one appropriate technology, not just “about” it).
  - provide appropriate instruction about how software tools work to make it easier to learn and use new tools over time.
  - Enable students to apply their learning in the creation of a digital artifact.
  - Enable students to explore at least three of the student learning outcomes, as specified in the syllabus.
  - Be designed so that at least 20% of the course grade is determined by Digital Literacy (DL) curricular elements.
  - Articulate the official Learning Outcomes (see above) in the course syllabus.

- **Student Requirements**: To receive credit for successful completion of a Digital Literacy (DL) course, students will:
  - Use digital technologies to achieve three or more of the student learning outcomes stated above.
  - Engage in advanced usage of software tools to produce a digital artifact of the work done in the class.
  - Demonstrate a critical understanding of how digital technologies impact our world in class discussions, in written essay form and/or in their creative or artistic work.

- **Additional Notes**: It is recommended that the course provide evidence in the syllabus of at least two instructional modes for supporting student learning, i.e., online tutorials and/or instructional videos as well as conceptual explanations or additional instructional support in the form of a senior/advanced student, another faculty member, or staff technology consultant.

## Assurance of Learning

Learning goals derived from the department’s mission statement drive the delivery of this course. The affirmation of learning will be ascertained, if applicable, through an evaluation rubric that meets the AACSB Assurance of Learning Standards.

## General Course Policies

- **CLASS PREPARATION**: Classes are designed to supplement the reading materials (textbooks, course slides, and required readings). Therefore, lectures will not necessarily address the text directly and may not cover all the details in the reading materials. Students must read assigned materials as part of the learning effort. Reading the assigned materials is a good way to cultivate your critical thinking ability and help better follow lectures. Bottom line: Read course slides and required readings.
- **PARTICIPATION**: Participation in classes is expected for all students, and a lack of participation will impact the grade. Students need to inform the instructor before the class about any anticipated excused absences. See the university attendance guidelines for the definition of excused absences.
- **LATE SUBMISSIONS**: DO NOT wait until the last minute to submit your assignments as it is always possible to encounter network or computer problems. You do have a grace period of TWO day for late submissions without any penalties, any late work after the grace period won’t be accepted. Please come to my office hour if you need help on lecture, assignments, and/or computer programming.
- **WRITING**: Professional writing is expected for all assignments, tests, or presentations. You can utilize the writing center if you need assistance. Grammarly and any other similar tools are recommended.
- **COMMUNICATION**: Course communication will be done through Canvas and/or email. Be sure to check both regularly for announcements, updates, etc. You are responsible for any content provided. Students need to inform the instructor before the class about any anticipated excused absences. See the university attendance guidelines for the definition of excused absences. Leaving the lecture early without permission is automatically an unexcused absence.

If you have unavoidable commitments that necessitate taking exams on different dates, please inform me well in advance. Note that such accommodations will be considered on a case-by-case basis and are not guaranteed.

Homework assignments are to be turned in at 11:59 PM on their due dates. **You are allowed to have a grace period of two day for late submissions**, but any late submissions after that will not be accepted. If there are extreme circumstances which prevent you from completing the assignment on time, please email me before the due date. BAT2301 - Statistics for Business
Lecture 10 Introduction to Discrete Probability Distributions - 2
Ian Liu
Department of Fin&BA
Trinity University
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 1/26
What Is Binomial Distribution? And When To Use
It?
A binomial distributionis a statistical tool to model the number of
successes in a fixed number of trials (or experiments) where each trial
has only two possible outcomes: success or failure.
Think of it as flipping a coin multiple times and counting how many
heads (successes) you get.
Use the binomial distribution when:
You want to model the number of times an event occurs (like getting
heads) in a fixed number of attempts (like flipping a coin).
You’re dealing with situations where there are only two possible
outcomes (success or failure) for each attempt.
You know the probability of success for each trial and want to find out
the likelihood of getting a specific number of successes.
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 2/26
Difference Between Uniform Distribution and
Binomial Distribution
Binomial Distribution: Used when you have a fixed number of trials,
each with two possible outcomes (success or failure). You’re interested
in the number of successes.
Example: Flipping a coin 10 times and counting how many heads
(successes) you get.
Uniform Distribution: Used when all outcomes are equally likely. Every
possible outcome has the same probability.
Example: Rolling a fair die, where each of the six faces (1, 2, 3, 4, 5, or
6) has an equal chance of showing up.
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 3/26
The Binomial Experiment
A binomial experiment has the following four properties.
1 The experiment consists of a sequence ofn identical trials.
2 Two outcomes, success and failure, are possible in each trial.
3 The stationarity assumption: the probability of a success, denoted byp,
and the probability of a failure, denoted by 1−p, do not change from
trial to trial.
4 The trials are independent.
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 4/26
The Binomial Experiment
Consider the binomial experiment of an insurance agent calling 10
customers.
We define a success if the agent closes the sale of insurance with a
customer, and a failure if he does not.
From past data/experience, we know that the agent closes on an
insurance in 10% of the calls,psuccess = 0.10.
The stationarity assumption requires that the probability of a success
(selling the insurance) remains the same over each trial(call).
The independence of the trials regards the decision of each customer of
whether to purchase or not the insurance.
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 5/26
The Binomial Experiment
In a binomial experiment, our interest is in the numberx of successes
occurring in then trials.
Because the number of successes is finite,x is adiscrete random
variable.
The probability distribution associated with this random variable is
called the binomial probability distribution,
And it is described by thebinomial probability function.

px (1−p)n−x provides the probability of a particular sequence of trial
outcomes with x successes in n trials
