Poisson Probability Distribution
A Poisson Probability Distribution describesx, the number of
occurrences over a specified interval of time or space, if the
following two properties of a Poisson experiment are respected
The probability of an occurrence is the same for any two intervals of
equal length.
The occurrence or nonoccurrence in any interval is independent of the
occurrence or nonoccurrence in any other interval.
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 17/26
Poisson Probability Distribution
The Poisson probability function is described by the following equation
f (x ) = µx e−µ
x !
Where:
x = a discrete random variable describing the number of occurrences
over an interval.
f (x ) = the probability of x occurrences in an interval
µ= expected value or mean number of occurrences in an interval
NOTE: In a Poisson probability distribution,σ2 = u (the variance is
equal to the mean.)
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 18/26
Poisson Probability Distribution Example
An analysis of historical data shows that patients arrive at the
emergency room of a large hospital on weekend mornings at the
average rateµ=10 over a 15-minute interval.
What is the probability of exactlyx = 5 arrivals in 15 minutes on a
weekend morning?
In a Poisson experiment, we have
f (5) = µx e−µ
x ! = 105e−10
5! = 0.0378 = 3.78%
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 19/26
Poisson Probability Distribution Example
What if we want to know the probability of exactlyx=1 arrival over a
3-minute interval?
We observe that, on average, over a 3-minute interval, we have
µ= 10
15×(3) = 2. Thus, the probability of one arrival over a 3-minute
period is
f (1) = µx e−µ
x ! = 21e−2
1! = 0.2707 = 27.07%
The probability of observing 5 arrivals over a 15-minute period is not
the same as observing 1 arrival over a 3-minute period.
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 20/26
Properties of the Poisson Probability Distribution
Because there is no stated upper limit for the number of occurrences,
the Poisson probability functionf (x ) is applicable for valuesx =
0,1,2,... without an upper limit.
In practical applications,x will eventually become large enough so that
f (x ) becomes approximately zero and the probability of any larger
values of x negligible.
A Poisson probability distribution has the variance equal to the mean:
µ=σ2
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 21/26
Why Are Mean and Variance the Same for Poisson?
Normally, we know that the variance represents how much the number
of events fluctuates around the mean. In other words, it shows how
much deviation we expect from the average number of occurrences.
For events that following the poisson distribution, rareness and
randomness are being modeled:
Cars speeding is a rare event in the sense that not every car speeds, but
over time, you’ll observe a consistent average.
A speeding car is a rare and random event, which is an independent
event as well.
The number of speeding cars in any given hour is independent of the
number in the previous or next hour (randomness).
That’s why we define them the same.
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 22/26
Properties of the Poisson Probability Distribution
Probabilities can be calculated using the Poisson Probability Table, if
you don’t have a TI-84 calculator.
For example, to obtain the probability ofx=5 arrivals over a 15-minute
period whenµ=10 using the table, follow these two steps:
First, scroll down the leftmost column until you findx=5
Second, move along the row forx = 5 until you reach the column
corresponding toµ= 10.
The answer is 0.0378.
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 23/26

equal to the mean?

We haveµ=10, the standard deviation,σ=

√
10 = 3.16

Over a 3-minute interval,
We haveµ= 2 andσ=
√

√
2 = 1.41
And a much larger coefficient of variation of1.41

That’s the reason why the probabilities are also very different.

Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/25/2024 5 / 18
What is Hypothesis Testing? And Why Do We
Need It?
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/25/2024 6 / 18
Hypothesis Testing
Hypothesis testing can be used to determine whether a statement
about the value of a population parameter should or should not be
rejected or believed to be true/false
The null hypothesis, denoted byH0 , is atentative assumption about
a population parameter
The alternative hypothesis, denoted byHa, is the opposite of what is
stated in the null hypothesis
The hypothesis testing procedure uses data from a sample to test the
two competing statements indicated byH0 and Ha
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/25/2024 7 / 18
Developing Null and Alternative Hypotheses
It could be tricky understand the context of the situation and
structure the hypotheses appropriately so that the test conclusion
provides the desired information.
All hypothesis testing applications involvecollecting a sample and
using the sample results to provide evidence for drawing a conclusion.
Good questions to consider when formulating the null and alternative
hypotheses are:
What is the purpose of collecting the sample?
What conclusions are we hoping to make?
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/25/2024 8 / 18
Developing Null and Alternative Hypotheses -
Example
When an attempt is being made to gather evidence in support of a
hypothesis, it is often best to begin with the alternative hypothesis
and make it the conclusion that the researcher hopes to support.
The conclusion that the research hypothesis is true is made if the
sample data provide sufficient evidence to show that the null
hypothesis can be rejected.
Example: A new fuel injection unit being developed is believed to
provide an MPG of more than 24 MPG.
We set the population mean miles per gallonHa :µ>24 as the
alternative hypothesis
And set the null hypothesis asH0 :µ≤24
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/25/2024 9 / 18
Developing Null and Alternative Hypotheses -
Example
We might begin with a belief or assumption that a statement about
the value of a population parameter is true.
We then use a hypothesis test to challenge the assumption and
determine if there is statistical evidence to conclude that the
assumption is incorrect.
Example: The label on an Ozarka water bottle states that it contains
16.9 fluid ounces.
We assume the label correct, provided the population mean filling
weight,µ, is 16.9 fluid ounces, and set it as the null hypothesis, or
H0 :µ= 16.9
Then, we challenge the assumption in the alternative hypothesis
Ha :µ̸= 16.9
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/25/2024 10 / 18
Summary of Forms for Null and Alternative
Hypotheses
Rule 1: The equality part of the hypotheses always appears in the null
hypothesis.
Rule 2: A hypothesis test about the value of a population meanµ
must take one of the followingTHREE forms, whereµ0 is the
hypothesized value of the population mean.
One-tailed (Lower-tail): H0 :µ≥µ0 vs. Ha :µ<µ0
One-tailed (Upper-tail): H0 :µ≤µ0 vs. Ha :µ>µ0
Two-tailed (Both Upper and Lower):H0 :µ=µ0 vs. Ha :µ̸=µ0
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/25/2024 11 / 18
Summary of Forms for Null and Alternative
Hypotheses
In this chapter, we show how statistical inference from sample data
and results can be used together with hypothesis testing to determine
whether a statement about thevalue of a population parameter
should or should not be rejected.
If the sample results support or favoriteHa, then we rejectH0
Otherwise, we do not rejectH0
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/25/2024 12 / 18
We Are Humans, We Make Mistakes/Wrong
Guesses.
Because hypothesis tests are based on sample data, we must allow for
the possibility of errors.
There are two types of error that can be made in hypothesis tests:
Type I and Type II errors.
Figure 1: Error Types
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/25/2024 13 / 18
Type I and Type II Errors
Figure 2: Error Types
Figure 3: Correct Predictions and
Error Types
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/25/2024 14 / 18
Type I and Type II Errors
Type I Error
A Type I error is rejectingH0 when it is true.
The probability of making a Type I error when the null hypothesis is
true as an equality is called the level of significance.
Applications of hypothesis testing that only control for the Type I error
are often called significance tests.
Type II Error
A Type II error is acceptingH0 when it is false.
Statisticians avoid the risk of making a Type II error by using “do not
reject H0” rather than “acceptH0”. - SNEAKY!!
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/25/2024 15 / 18
Example of Type I and Type II Errors
Recall the hypothesis testing example about the new fuel injection
unit. Imagine you’ve developed a new fuel system for cars, and you
believe it makes cars more fuel-efficient than the current standard.
You decide to test if this new system really improves the car’s miles
per gallon (MPG) rating compared to the usual 24 MPG benchmark.
H0 :µ≤24
Ha :µ>24 (1)
Type I Error: This is like sounding a false alarm. It’s when you
conclude that your new fuel system does indeed improve the car’s
MPG (makes it better than 24 MPG), but in reality, it doesn’t — it’s
just the same as the old system. So, you mistakenly think you’ve
made an improvement when you haven’t.
Type II Error: This is like missing something important. It’s when you
decide that your new fuel system doesn’t make any difference (so,
MPG is 24 or worse), but in fact, it actually does improve the MPG.
So, you’ve overlooked a real improvement, thinking your system
didn’t make the car more fuel-efficient when it really did.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/25/2024 16 / 18
Example of Type I and Type II Errors
In summary, a Type I error is thinking you’ve achieved something
special when you haven’t, and a Type II error is failing to recognize an
actual achievement, thinking nothing has changed.
Type I Error: Thinking your new system works (improves MPG) when
it doesn’t.
Type II Error: Not realizing your new system works (actually improves
MPG) when it does.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/25/2024 17 / 18
One-Tailed Test About a Population Mean
One-tailed tests about a population mean take one of the following
two forms.
Lower Tail Test
H0 :µ≥µ0
Ha :µ<µ0
(2)
One-tailed tests about a population mean take one of the following
two forms.
Upper Tail Test
H0 :µ≤µ0
Ha :µ>µ0
(3)
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 03/25/2024 18 / 18

Introduction
In this chapter, we extend the study of probability by introducing the
concepts of random variables and probability distributions.
Random variables and probability distributions are models for
populations of data.
The values of random variables represent the values of the data
The probability distribution provides either the probability of each data
value or a rule for computing the probability of each data value or a set
of data values
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 4/28

Difference?
Definition: discrete datainvolves distinct, countable values. It can
only take specific numbers (whole numbers) with no in-betweens!
Examples:
Number of pets (You can have 1, 2, or 3 pets, but not 2.5 pets)
Number of cars (You can have 1, 2, and 3 cars, but not 4.5 cars)
Number of ex’s

Discrete vs. Continuous Data: What’s the
Difference?
Definition: continuous data can take any value within a range,
including fractions and decimals.
Examples:
Gas prices ($6.55/Gallon in California and $2.47/Gallon in Texas)
Time to finish 5K run (Your race time could be 15 minutes , or 15.66
minutes)
Temperature of this classroom (It could weigh 71.5F or 21.9C)

experiment.
A discrete random variablemay assume
Either a finite number of values or an infinite sequence of values.
A continuous random variablemay assume
Any numerical value in an interval or collection of intervals.

How probabilities are distributed over the values of the random variable.

Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 13/28
The Two Types of Discrete Probability Distributions
Uses the rules of assigning probabilities to experimental outcomes to
determine probabilities for each value of the random variable.
E.g. Flipping a coin, tossing a die
Uses a special mathematical formula to compute the probabilities for
each value of the random variable.
E.g. # of buses/customers/calls coming between 1:00PM and 3:00PM
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 14/28
Probability Function
The probability function, denoted byf (x ), defines the probability
distribution by providing the probability for each value of the random
variable.
Think of this function as a family name or just probabilityp(x )
Contains or describes a group of members
The required conditions for a discrete probability function are:
f (x )≥0 and
∑
f (x ) = 1
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 15/28
Ways to Assign Probabilities to RVs

data or the outcomes of experiments. The use of the relative frequency
method to develop discrete probability distributions leads to an
empirical discrete distribution.

The expected valueor mean, of a random variable is a measure of its
central location.
The expected value of a discrete random variable is calculated as a
weighted average of the values the random variable may assume. The
weights are the probabilities.

xf (x )

Let’s Work On An Example Together
Interpretations of Mean(Expected Value), Variance, and S.D.
On average, a Trinity student drinks about 1.31 cups of coffee per day.
This does not mean a typical student drinks exactly 1.31 cups. Instead,
it represents the long-run average consumption if we averaged coffee
consumption across many students.
The variance measures how spread out students’ coffee consumption is
around the mean. A variance of 1.71 indicates that there is moderate
variability in daily coffee consumption among Trinity students.
The standard deviationtells us the typical distance between a
student’s coffee consumption and the average consumption.A standard
deviation of about 1.31 cupsmeans that:
A student’s daily coffee intake typically differs from the average by about
1.3 cups. Since the mean is 1.31 cups,most students drink roughly
within the following range, which also aligns with the data where most
students report drinking 0, 1, or 2 cups per day:
mean ±S.D.= 1.31±1.31 = [0→2.6 cups/day]

Question 1

31 received an average refund of $1056 in 2018. Consider the population of “last-minute” filers who mail
their tax return during the last five days of the income tax period (typically April 10 to April 15).

individuals receive lower refunds than do early filers. Develop appropriate hypotheses such that
rejection ofH0 will support the researcher’s contention.
b. For a sample of 400 individuals who filed a tax return between April 10 and 15, the sample mean
refund was $910. Based on prior experience a population standard deviation ofσ= $1, 600 may be
assumed. What is the p-value?
c. Atα= 0.05, what is your conclusion?
d. Repeat the preceding hypothesis test using the critical value approach.

Numerical Measures
If the measures are computed for data from a sample, they are called
sample statistics.
What is it?
If the measures are computed for data from a population, they are
called population parameters or population statistics.
What is it?
A sample statistic is referred to as thepoint estimatorof the
corresponding population parameter.
What is it?
Ian Liu Department of Finance and Business Analytics Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 3/22
Population and Sample
Population
Bigger group or the entire group
All Trinity students
All college students in Texas
All college students in the nation
Sample
Trinity students who are FIN or BAT majored
Trinity students who are taking BAT2301 with Ian in Spring 26
Why Sample vs. Population?
Usually, not practical or even possible to work with the entire population
Thus, we want to use sample data to infer/guess how the population
would “looks” like

Mean
Perhaps the most important measure of location is themean.
The mean provides a measure ofcentral location.
The mean of a data set is the average of all the data values.
The sample mean¯x is the point estimator of the population mean,µ.
¯x =
∑ xi
n
Where ∑ xi = the sum of the values ofn observations and n = the number
of observations in the sample

¯x represents thesample mean

Discussion
Imagine you manage a sales team, and you want to calculate the
average revenue per salesperson to determine the annual
bonus/commission. Who is a better salesperson?
Not all salespeople have the same number of clients or the same sales
targets.

accounts.
Salesperson A manages 5 clients, contributing a total revenue of $50,000.
Salesperson B manages 15 clients, contributing a total revenue of
$150,000.
Salesperson C manages 10 clients, contributing a total revenue of
$200,000.
Ian Liu Department of Finance and Business Analytics Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/22
Discussion
Can we just find the average revenue per salesperson bydivide the
total revenue by the number of salespeople?
Why or why not?
In this case, using a weighted mean would give you a more accurate
representation of the average revenue per salesperson by taking into
account the size or value of each salesperson’s portfolio.
Let’s use the weighted mean to consider the contribution of each
salesperson based on their number of clients.

Weighted Mean
In some instances the mean is computed by giving each observation a
weight that reflects its relative importance.
The choice of weights depends on the application.
The weights might be the number of credit hours earned for each
grade, as in GPA.
In other weighted mean computations, quantities such as pounds,
dollars, or volume are frequently used.
¯x =
∑ wi xi∑ wi
Where xi = value of observationi, and wi weight got observationi.

Summary About Different Ways to Find the Mean
Simple Mean: you use the simple mean when all data points
contribute equally to the overall picture, like calculating the average
temperature of a city over a week.
Weighted Mean: you use the weighted mean when some data points
are more influential than others, like calculating the average grade in a
class where assignments have different weights or significance.
Ian Liu Department of Finance and Business Analytics Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 16/22
Percentiles
A percentile provides information about how the data are spread over
the interval from the smallest value to the largest value.
Admission test scores for colleges and universities are frequently
reported in terms of percentiles.
The pth percentile of a data set is a value such that at least p percent
of the items take on this value or less and at least(100 − p) percent of
the items take on this value or more.
Arrange the data in ascending order.
Compute Lp, which is the location of thepth percentile:
Lp = ( p
100)(n +1)
Ian Liu Department of Finance and Business Analytics Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 17/22
How to Find Percentiles
Figure 5: Percentile Example
Let’s find 80th percentile?
Example: Apartment Rents: Lp = ( p
100 )(n +1) = 80
100 (70 +1) = 56.8
56.8 is a number between 56 and 57
The 80th percentile is the 56th value plus 0.8 times the difference
between the 57th and 56th values.
Thus, the 80th percentile = 635+0.8(649 −635) = 646.2
Ian Liu Department of Finance and Business Analytics Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 18/22
Interpretations of the Percentiles
Figure 6: Percentile Example
At least 80% of the tenants pay a rent of 646.2 or less.
At least 20% of the tenants pay a rent of 646.2 or more.
Ian Liu Department of Finance and Business Analytics Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 19/22
Quartiles
It’s often desireable to divide a dataset into FOUR parts, with each
part containing approximately 1/4 or 25% of the data points(aka.
observations)
Quartiles are specific percentiles.
First Quartile = 25th Percentile
Second Quartile = 50th Percentile = Median
Third Quartile = 75th Percentile

Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/07/2025 3/40
Why Interval Estimation?
You don’t want to give an overly confident answer for something you
are not very sure.
It’s like a way of giving a smart guess with a buffer zone, backed with
statistical evidence, knowing you won’t always be 100% precise, but
you’ll be close enough most of the time.
Imagine you’re launching a new product, and you’re trying to guess
how much profit it will make. An interval estimate is like saying, “We
don’t know exactly, but we estimate the profit will be between $50,000
and $80,000.”
You are going out with friends and your parents ask you when you are
gonna be home? Say something-ish, not an exact time.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/07/2025 4/40
Introduction
A point estimator is a sample statistic used to estimate a population
parameter.
The sample mean¯x is a point estimator of the population meanµ
The sample proportionˆp is a point estimator of the population
proportion p
The sampling distributions of¯x and ˆp play key roles in computing
these interval estimates.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/07/2025 5/40
Introduction
An interval estimateis computed by adding and subtracting a
value(called the margin of error) to the point estimate. Thus, the
general forms of an interval estimate of a population mean and
proportion are:
¯x ±Margin of Error and ˆp ±Margin of Error
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/07/2025 6/40
Margin of Error and the Interval Estimate
A point estimatorcannot be expected to provide the exact value of
the population parameter.
But we want to try hard to get closer and closer to the exact
value/truth.
An interval estimatecan be computed by adding and subtracting a
margin of error to the point estimate.
The purpose of an interval estimate is to provide information about
how close the point estimate is to the value of the parameter.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/07/2025 7/40
Interval Estimate of a Population Mean: When the
σ Is Known
In order to develop an interval estimate of a population mean, the
margin of error must be computed using either:
The population standard deviationσ, or
The sample standard deviations
σis rarely KNOWN exactly(why?). But often a good estimate can be
obtained based on historical data and/or other information available
We refer to such cases as theσKNOWN case
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/07/2025 8/40

n = 100 customers, a simple random sample

s =σ= 20, based on historical data
The population follows a normal distribution
Calculations reveal that the sample mean
a point estimate of population meanµ, is: ¯x = $82.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/07/2025 9/40
A New Greek Letterα
αis the significance level(or the level of significance) and is used to
calculate a confidence interval(which is often given).
The confidence level is calculated as 100∗(1−α).
It represents the probability of obtaining certain results by chance.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/07/2025 10/40

An interval estimate of the population meanµ, for theσknown case,
can be written as
¯x±zα/2
σ√n
Where, 1−αis the confidence coefficient, andzα/2(the z value
providing an area ofα/2 in the upper tail of the standard normal
probability distribution.

σ¯x = σ√n = 20√

Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/07/2025 12/40
Sampling Distribution for the Sample Mean
This sampling distribution depicts how values of¯x are distributed
around the population meanµ
It follows that 95% of the values of the normally distributed¯x random
variable falls within±1.96σ¯x standard deviations of the mean, or
within
±1.96σ¯x =±1.96(2) =±3.92
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/07/2025 13/40
The Confidence Level
Let us set the margin of error equal to 3.92 and compute the interval
estimate ofµusing ¯x±3.92.
The figure in the next slide shows what could happen if we took three
separate samples of 100 Lloyd’s customers and built an interval
estimate for each of them.
The first two intervals, built around the sample means¯x1 and ¯x2,
include the population mean,µ, but the third one,¯x3, does not.
It turns out that 95% of all intervals formed by subtracting 3.92 from
¯x and adding 3.92 to¯x will include the population mean,µ
We call an interval built around¯x a confidence interval established at
the 95% confidence level.

contain the population mean.

Practical Advice(You May Get Asked by An
Interviewer)
Adequate Sample Size
The sampling distribution of¯x is normal if the underlying population
distribution is normal; otherwise, a sample size ofn≥30 is required.
If the population distribution is highly skewed or contains outliers, a
sample size of 50 or more is recommended.
If the population is not normally distributed but is roughly symmetric,
a sample size as small as 15 will suffice.
If the population is believed to be at least approximately normal, a
sample size of less than 15 can be used.
For small sample sizes, a histogram of the sample data should be built
to learn about the shape of the population distribution.

Why Interval Estimation?

Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/07/2025 24/40
Population Mean: σ Unknown
The t Distribution
If an estimate of the population standard deviation,σ, cannot be
developed prior to sampling, we use the sample standard deviation,s,
instead.
This is referred to as theσunknown case
With the interval estimate ofµbased on thet distribution, which is a
family of similar probability distributions depending on a parameter
called the degrees of freedom.
A t distribution with more degrees of freedom has less dispersion.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/07/2025 25/40
What is the Degree of Freedom?
Degrees of Freedom refer to the number of independent pieces of
information (data points) that go in the computation of the sample
standard deviation, s, and with thenth data point being “locked” by
the computations of the sample mean,¯x, needed for the computation
of s.
n−1 is how we determine the degree of freedom
Why n−1?
Imagine you’re at a party with a group of friends, and you’ve decided
to line up for a photo. If the photographer asks everyone to spread out
evenly across the frame, the first few friends can choose their spots
freely, but the last friend’s position is determined by everyone else’s
choice to maintain the balance.
In statistics, this is like havingn−1 degrees of freedom: forn friends,
n−1 can choose their spot freely, while the last spot is constrained by
the overall arrangement,n−1 data points are free to vary, but the last
is determined by the sum needed to meet the mean.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/07/2025 26/40
Population Mean: σ Unknown
As the degrees of freedom increase, the difference between thet
distribution and the standard normal probability distribution becomes
smaller and smaller
For more than 100 degrees of freedom, the standard normal z value
provides a good approximation to thet value
Keep this in mind: if the degrees of freedom exceed 100, the standard
normal z value provides a good approximation to the t value.
Figure 3: Standard Normal Distribution andt distribution

s√n

upper tail of at distribution with n−1 degrees of freedom

Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 4/33
Why Do We Need Probability?
Managers often base their decisions on an analysis of uncertainties
such as:
What are the chances that the sales will decrease if we increase prices?
What is the likelihood a new assembly method will increase productivity?
How likely is it that the project will be finished on time?
What is the chance that a new investment will be profitable?
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 5/33
Probability
Recall that in statistics, we use sample data to infer the nature of a
population.
Probability assumes that the population isknown and calculate the
chances of obtaining various samples from the population.
Thus
Probability is the reverse of statistics,
In probability, we use the population information to infer the probable
nature of the sample.
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 6/33
Probability Definitions
Probability is a numerical measure of the likelihood that an event will
occur. Probability values are always assigned on a scale from 0 to
1(0≤prob≤1):
A probability near zero indicates an event is quite unlikely to occur.
A probability near one indicates an event is almost certain to occur.
Other probabilities between 0 and 1 represent degrees of likelihood that
an event will occur.
The probabilities of all the sample points within a sample space must
sum to 1: ∑ probi = 1
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 7/33
Randomness
Randomness refers to the lack of pattern or predictability in events. A
random sequence of events has no order and does not follow some
pattern or combination.
Rolling a die, flipping a coin, weather patterns, stock market
fluctuations.
We still try to have some control over the randomnesses -probability
is a tool to understand those randomnesses:
Understanding randomness is key to interpreting data, predicting
phenomena, and designing systems in various scientific and practical
fields.
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 8/33
Random Experiment
A Random experiment is a process that generates experimental
outcomes(data), and possesses the following properties:
The experimental outcomes are well-defined and may also be listed prior
to conducting the experiment.
On any single repetition (trial) of the experiment, one and only one of
the possible experimental outcomes will occur.
The experimental outcome that occurs on any trial is determined solely
by chance.
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 9/33

Say if I’m going to ask 10 of you to predict a toss, and then toss a die
to get the real data.
You win the game if your prediction and actual toss are the same.
You will be randomly called and asked to predict the outcome of each
toss. While making predictions:
1 Looking for patterns in the data, and
2 Based on the previous tosses, what do you think will happen next?

Actual
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 11/33
Gambler’s Fallacy
This experiment highlights the fact that many people fall into the
Gambler’s Fallacy, which people always mistakenly believe that the
probability of an event happening changes based on the outcome of
past events.
For example, someone might believe that if a dice has landed on 3 five
times in a row, the next toss is more likely to land on 3, or NOT likely
to land on 3.
However, the probability of each number on a dice always remains the
same, no matter how many times a number has appeared before.
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 12/33
Random Experiment and Sample Space
An experimental outcome is also called asample point. The sample
space, S, for an experiment is the set of all experimental outcomes.
Example: consider the random experiment of tossing a coin.
The experimental outcomes (sample points) areHead and Tail.
The sample space is:s ={Head, Tail}
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 13/33

representation that helps visualizing a multiple-step experiment.

2 Observe a tail
This sample space in set notation:S :{H, T}
Observe the up faces on two coins.
1 Observe{HH}
2 Observe{HT}
3 Observe{TH}
4 Observe{TT}
5 Or this sample space in set notation:S :{HH, HT , TH, TT}
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 15/33
Counting Rule for Multiple-Step Experiments
A multiple-step experiment can be described as a sequence ofk steps
with n1 possible outcomes on the first step,n2 possible outcomes on
the second step, and so on. (n1, n2, n3, ..., nk)
As an example, consider the random experiment of tossing two coins,
one at a time. The first tossed coin hasn1 = 2 outcomes, and the
second tossed coin has alson2 = 2 outcomes.
Thus, we have: 1st toss∗2nd toss = 2∗2 = 4 experimental outcomes

overall completion goal of 10 months.

total of 3∗3 = 9 experimental outcomes.

The product of all positive integers up to a given number

C 5

12 = 10

Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 23/33
Counting Rule for Permutations Example
Consider again the inspector’s quality control process. In a group of
five parts, how many permutations of two parts may be selected?

P 5

Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 24/33
Why Combination
Use combinations when the order doesn’t matter.
The Problem: The CEO needs to form a sub-committee of 3 members
to review the annual budget.
Why it’s a Combination: If you select Alice, Bob, and Charlie, they are
the committee. If you select Charlie, Bob, and Alice, it is the exact
same committee. The order in which you pick them is irrelevant
because they all have the same “rank” or role within that group.
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 25/33
Why Permutation?
Use permutations when the order matters.
The Problem: The same Board of Directors needs to elect three
specific officers: a President, a Vice President, and a Treasurer.
Why it’s a Permutation: If you select Alice, Bob, and Charlie, the
outcome depends entirely on where they are placed:
Scenario A: Alice (President), Bob (VP), Charlie (Treasurer)
Scenario B: Charlie (President), Alice (VP), Bob (Treasurer)
Scenario C, etc.
Even though it is the same three people, Scenario A is a completely
different business outcome than Scenario B. Because the slots are
distinct (hierarchical), the order is critical.
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 26/33
Difference Between Combination and Permutation?
Both are to determine how many groups of objects from the same set
can be made
Use Permutation when the ordermatters
If the order DOESN’T matter, useCombination
The counting rule for permutations is crosely related to the one for
combinations because every selection ofx objects can be ordered inx !
different ways.
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 27/33
Difference Between Combination and Permutation?
Given the letters A, B, C, and D, list the permutations and
combinations for selecting two letters.
Permutation: AB, BA, CA, DA, AC, BC, CB, DB, AD, BD, CD, DC
Combination: AB, BA, CA, DA, AC, BC,CB, DB, AD, BD, CD,DC

Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 29/33
Assigning Probabilities
The basic requirements for assigning probabilities are
The probability assigned to each experimental outcome must be between
0 and 1, inclusively.
The sum of the probabilities for all experimental outcomes must equal 1.

Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 10/77
Statistical Experiments vs. Other Physical Science
Experiments
In statistics, the notion of an experiment differs somewhat from the
notion of an experiment in the physical sciences.
In the physical sciences, researchers usually conduct an experiment in a
laboratory or a controlled environment in order to learn about cause
and effect.
In statistical experiments, probability determines outcomes.
Even though the experiment is repeated in exactly the same way, an
entirely different outcome may occur.
Because of this influence of probability on the outcome, the
experiments of statistics are sometimes called random experiments.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 11/77

space, S, for an experiment is the set of all experimental outcomes.

The experimental outcomes (sample points) areHead and Tail.
The sample space is:s ={Head, Tail}

Assigning Probabilities
The basic requirements for assigning probabilities are
The probability assigned to each experimental outcome must be
between 0 and 1, inclusively.
The sum of the probabilities for all experimental outcomes must equal
1.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 29/77
Assigning Probabilities
Three methods of assigning probabilities:
The classical method, is based on the assumption of equally likely
outcomes.
The empirical method, based on relative frequencies from experimental
or historical data.
The subjective method, based on experience or intuition when no data
are available.

Events
An event is a collection of sample points.
If we can identify all the sample points of an experiment and assign a
probability to each, we can compute the probability of an event.
1604 Project was an example

Events
Texas DoT was interested in the probability that the project is
completed in 10 months or less.
From the table in the previous slide, we see that there are six sample
points that provide a project completion time of 10 months or less.

less. Thus, we have:
C = {(2, 6), (2, 7), (2, 8), (3, 6), (3, 7), (4, 6)}
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 35/77

Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 36/77
Probability of An Event
The probability of an event is equal to the sum of the probabilities of
the sample points in the event.
We can calculate the probability that the 1604 project is completed in
10 months or lessby adding the probabilities of the six sample
points belonging to eventC.
P(C) = P(2,6) + P(2,7) + P(2,8) + P(3,6) + P(3,7) + P(4,6)
P(C) = 0.15 +0.15 +0.05 +0.10 +0.20 +0.05 = 0.7
Similarly, the probability that the 1604 project is completed inless
than 10 monthsis
P(L) = P(2,6) + P(2,7) + P(3,6) = 0.15 +0.15 +0.10 = 0.4
And the probability that the 1604 project is completed inmore than
10 monthsis
P(M) = P(3,8) + P(4,7) + P(4,8) = 0.05 +0.10 +0.15 = 0.3

Some Basic Relationships of Probability
In an experiment with a large number of sample points, the
identification of all the sample points, as well as the determination of
their associated probabilities, can become an extremely cumbersome
task.
In this section, we present some basic probability relationships that
can be used to compute the probability of an event without
knowledge of all the sample point probabilities.
The complement of an event
The union of two events
The intersection of two events
The addition law
The multiplication law

Some Basic Relationships of Probability
In an experiment with a large number of sample points, the
identification of all the sample points, as well as the determination of
their associated probabilities, can become an extremely cumbersome
task.
In this section, we present some basic probability relationships that
can be used to compute the probability of an event without
knowledge of all the sample point probabilities.
The complement of an event
The union of two events
The intersection of two events
The addition law
The multiplication law
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 41/77
Complement of an Event
The complement of eventA is defined as the event consisting of all
sample points that are not inA
The complement ofA is denoted byAC
In any probability application, either eventA or its complementAC
must occur, as shown in the Venn diagram
Figure 5: Venn Diagram
Therefore, we have
P(A) = P(AC ) = 1orP(A) = 1−P(AC )
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 42/77
Complement of an Event
The complement event of an eventA can also be denoted as¯A, which
consists of all outcomes that are not in A. Namely event¯A occurs if A
does not occur.
Example: Roll a die:E1 ={1,3,5}. Then ¯E1 ={2,4,6}
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 43/77
Union of Two Events
The union of eventsA and B is the event containing all sample points
that are inA and B or both.
The union of eventsA and B is denoted byA∪B.
Note that the two circles in the Venn diagram overlap, indicating that
some sample points are contained in bothA and B .
Figure 6: Venn Diagram - Union
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 44/77
Union of Two Events
Union: The union event of two eventsA and B is denoted asA∪B,
which consists of all outcomes in eitherA or B or in both. Namely
event A∪B occurs if eitherA or B occurs.
Example: Roll a die:E1 ={1,3,5}and E2 ={1,2,3}. Then
E1∪E2 ={1,2,3,5}
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 45/77
Intersection of Two Events
The intersection of eventsA and B is the event containing all sample
points belonging to bothA and B. The intersection of eventsA and
B is denoted byA∩B.
Note that the area in the Venn diagram where the two circles overlap
is the intersection.
The intersection contains all the sample points that are in bothA and
B.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 46/77
Intersection of Two Events
The intersection event of two eventsA and B is denoted asA∩B,
which consists of all outcomes in bothA and B or in both.
A∩B occurs if bothA and B occur.
Example: Roll a die:E1 ={1,3,5}and E2 ={1,2,3}, then
E1∩E2 ={1,3}
Two eventsA and B are mutually exclusive ifA∩B =∅.
Figure 7: Venn Diagram - Intersection
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 47/77
Addition Law
The addition law provides a way to compute the probability of the
union of two events:
P(A∪B) = P(A) + P(B) + P(A∩B)
To understand the addition law intuitively, consider the following:
The sample points in the intersectionA∩B are in bothA and B.
When we computeP(A) + P(B)we are in effect counting each of the
sample points inA∩B twice.
We correct for this overcounting by subtractingP(A∩B)
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 48/77
Addition Law: Example
Toss two fair coins in very trial and assume that the four possible
outcomes S ={HH,HT ,TH,TT}are equally likely to happen.
Let A ={HH,HT}be the event that the first coin falls head, and
B ={HH,TH}be the event that the second coin falls head.
Problem: Find the probability that either the first coin falls head or
the second coin falls head?
Solution:
P(A∪B) = P(A) + P(B)−P(A∩B) = 1
2 + 1
2−1
4 = 3
4
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 49/77
An Application of the Addition Law
Consider a group of 50 software engineers who work in online banking.
At the end of an evaluation period, a bank manager found that
5 engineers completed work late (eventL), or P(L) = 5/50 = 0.10
6 engineers produced code that contains errors (eventE), or
P(E) = 6/50 = 0.12
2 engineers completed work late and produced code that contains
errors (event L∩E), or P(L∩E) = 2/50 = 0.04
The probability that an engineer completed work late or produced
code that contains errors (eventL∪E) can be calculated using the
addition law as
P(L∪E) = P(L) + P(E)−P(L∩E) = 0.10 +0.12−0.04 = 0.18
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 50/77
Mutually Exclusive Events
Two events are said to be mutually exclusive when they have no
sample points in common.
In other words, eventsA and B are mutually exclusive if, when one
event occurs, the other cannot occur.
Thus, the intersection ofA and B does not contain any points, and
P(A∩B) = 0.
When two events are mutually exclusive, the addition law simplifies to
P(A∪B) = P(A) + P(B)
Figure 8: Venn Diagram - Mutually Exclusive Events
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 51/77
Conditional Probability
We use the notationP(A|B) to denote the conditional probability of
event A, given that eventB has occurred.
We calculate the probability of eventA given B as
P(A|B) = P(A∩B)
P(B)
Figure 9: Venn Diagram - Conditional Events
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 52/77
Conditional Probability
We calculate the probability of eventB given A as
P(B|A) = P(A∩B)
P(A)
Therefore, P(A|B) and P(B|A) are NOT the same thing.

officers enables us to summarize the available information on

In the body of the table, we have the joint probabilities, which are the
intersection probabilities of the events of whether a police officer was

The joint probability that an officer was promotedA and is a maleM
P(A∩M) = 288/1200 = 0.24

The probability that an officer is maleM

Conditional Probability Analysis
We note that the marginal probabilities are found by summing the
joint probabilities in the corresponding row or column of the joint
probability table.
P(A) = P(A∩M) + P(A∩F ) = 0.24−0.03 = 0.27
The conditional probability that an officer was promotedA given that
the officer is maleM
P(A|M) = P(A capM )
P(M) = 288/1200
960/1200 = 288
960 = 0.30
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 58/77
Conditional Probability Analysis
The conditional probability that an officer was promotedA given that
the officer is femaleF
P(A|F ) = P(A capF )
P(F ) = 36/1200
240/1200 = 36
240 = 0.15
Thus, because P(A|M) > P(A|F ), or 0.3 > 0.15, the analysis
supports the female officers’ argument.

Independent Events
Two events A and B are independent if
P(A|B) = P(A) or P(B|A) = P(B)
Otherwise, the events are dependent if
P(A|B)̸= P(A) or P(B|A)̸= P(B)
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 60/77
Independent Events vs. Mutually Exclusive Events
Mutually exclusive events could refer to choosing between two
investment options
Such as investing in either stocks or bonds with a limited budget, where
selecting one option precludes investing in the other at the same time.
Independent events might involve the scenario where the performance
of a stock in the technology sector is unaffected by a change in
interest rates
Meaning the stock’s performance and the interest rate movement do
not influence each other’s outcomes.
Figure 12: Mutually Exclusive vs. Independent Events
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 61/77
Independent Events
In the analysis of the promotion of police officers, we found
P(A) = 0.27,P(A|M) = 0.30,P(A|F ) = 0.15
Because P(A|M)̸= P(A), that is, the probability of whether an
officer is promotedA is altered or affected by knowing whether the
officer is maleM, we conclude that the eventsA and M are
dependent or not independent.
Similarly, becauseP(A|F )̸= P(A), we would say that eventsA and F
are also dependent.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 62/77
Independent Events
A quick note:
Do not confuse the notion of mutually exclusive events with that of
independent events.
Two events with nonzero probabilities cannot be both mutually
exclusive and independent.
If one mutually exclusive event is known to occur, the other cannot
occur.
Thus, the probability of the other event occurring is reduced to 0.
Therefore, the two events are dependent.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 63/77
Multiplication Law
The multiplication law is based on the definition of conditional
probability, and it provides a way to compute the probability of the
intersection of two events
P(A∩B) = P(A|B)P(B) or P(B∩A) = P(B|A)P(A)
In the case of independent eventsP(A|B) = P(A) or P(B|A) = P(B),
and the multiplication law for independent eventsbecomes
P(A∩B) = P(A)P(B) or P(B∩A) = P(B)P(A)
Therefore, two eventsA and B are dependent when
P(A∩B)̸= P(A)P(B)
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 64/77
Multiplication Law
Example: Draw three cardsWITH replacement i.e., draw one card,
look at it, p ut it back, and re p eat twice more.
Problem: Find the probability of drawing 3 Queens in a row
Solution: Let Qi(i = 1,2,3) be the event thati th draw gives you a
Queen. Then the three events are independent of each other.
P(Q1∩Q2∩Q3) = P(Q1)P(Q2)P(Q3) = 4
52×4
52×4
52 = 0.00046
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 65/77
Multiplication Law
Example: Draw three cardsWITHOUT replacement i.e., draw one
card, look at it, keep it, and repeat for 2 more times
Problem: Find the probability of drawing 3 Queens in a row
Solution: Let Qi(i = 1,2,3) be the event thati th draw gives you a
Queen. Then the three events are dependent of each other.
P(Q1∩Q2∩Q3) = P(Q1)P(Q2|Q1)P(Q3|Q1∩Q2) = 4
52×3
51×2
50 = 0.00018

Prior and Posterior Probabilities
Often, we begin probability analysis with initial orprior probabilities.
Then, from a sample, special report, or a product test we obtain some
additional information.
Given this information, we calculate revised orposterior probabilities.
Bayes’ theorem provides the means for revising the prior probabilities.
Figure 13: Bayes’ Process
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 67/77
Bayes’ Theorem
Given an event B, and a set of mutually exclusive and exhaustive
events A1,A2, ...,An, and we know the prior probabilities
P(A1),P(A2), ...,P(An). Then the posterior is:
P(A1|B) = P(B|A1)P(A1)
P(B|A1)P(A1) + ...+ P(B|An)P(An)
Figure 14: Bayes’ Theorem
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 68/77
Bayes’ Theorem: Example
Example: 25 percent of residents in an area leaves their garage doors
open when they left their home. The chances of being raided are 5
percent and 1 percent for those who leave their doors open, and who
do not leave their doors open, respectively.
Problem: Suppose one resident was robbed, what is the probability
that he or she originally left his or her door open?
Solution: Let B = robbed residents,A1 = open doors residents,A2
= close door residents. We know that
P(A1) = 0.25,P(A2) = 0.75,P(B|A1) = 0.05,P(B|A2) = 0.01.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 02/05/2024 69/77

Figure 15: Bayes’ Theorem: Tree Diagram

Therefore
P(A1|B) = P(B|A1)P(A1)
P(B|A1)P(A1) + P(B|A2)P(A2)
P(A1|B) = 0.05(0.25)
0.05(0.25) +0.01(0.75)
P(A1|B) = 0.0125
0.02 = 0.625

Bayes’ Theorem: Another Example
Example: An insurance company classifies drivers as good, medium,
or poor risks. Drivers who apply to for insurance fall into these three
groups in the proportions: 30%, 50%, and 20%, respectively. The
probabilities of a good-risk, medium-risk, and poor-risk drivers will
have an accident are 0.01, 0.03, and 0.10, respectively.
Problem: Suppose the company sells Mr. Brophy an insurance policy
and he has an accident. What is the probability that Mr. Brophy was
a good-risk driver?
Solution: Let B = accident drivers,A1 = good drivers,A2 = medium
drivers, and A3 = poor drivers. We know thatP(A1) = 0.3,P(A2) =
0.5,P(A3) = 0.2; P(B|A1) = 0.01,P(B|A2) = 0.03,P(B|A3) = 0.10.

Independent Events
Two events A and B are independent if
P(A|B) = P(A) or P(B|A) = P(B)
Otherwise, the events are dependent if
P(A|B)̸= P(A) or P(B|A)̸= P(B)
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 2 / 34
More About Independent Events
Definition: Two events are independent if the occurrence of one event
does not affect the probability of the other occurring.
The Scenario: Imagine you are flipping a coin and rolling a standard
6-sided die.
Event A: The coin lands on Heads.
Event B: The die lands on 4.
Why it is Independent: The coin has no memory or connection to the
die. Getting “Heads” does not make it more or less likely that the die
will roll a “4.” The two systems are physically separate.
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 3 / 34
More About Mutually Exclusive Events
Definition: Two events are mutually exclusive if they cannot happen at
the same time. If one happens, the other strictly cannot.
The Scenario: Imagine you are rolling a single die one time.
Event A: You roll a 2.
Event B: You roll a 5.
Why it is Mutually Exclusive: You cannot roll a 2 and a 5 on the same
toss of a single die. The occurrence of Event A makes Event B
impossible for that specific trial. In a Venn diagram, these circles
would never touch (they are disjoint).
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 4 / 34
Independent Events vs. Mutually Exclusive Events
Mutually exclusive events could refer to choosing between two
investment options
Such as investing in either stocks or bonds with a limited budget, where
selecting one option precludes investing in the other at the same time.
Independent events might involve the scenario where the performance
of a stock in the technology sector is unaffected by a change in interest
rates
Meaning the stock’s performance and the interest rate movement do not
influence each other’s outcomes.
Figure 1: Mutually Exclusive vs. Independent Events
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 5 / 34
Independent Events
In the analysis of the promotion of police officers, we found
P(A) = 0.27, P(A|M) = 0.30, P(A|F ) = 0.15
Because P(A|M)̸= P(A), that is, the probability of whether an officer
is promoted A is altered or affected by knowing whether the officer is
male M, we conclude that the eventsA and M are dependent or not
independent.
Similarly, becauseP(A|F )̸= P(A), we would say that eventsA and F
are also dependent.
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 6 / 34
Independent Events
A quick note:
Do not confuse the notion of mutually exclusive events with that of
independent events.
Two events with nonzero probabilities cannot be both mutually exclusive
and independent.
If one mutually exclusive event is known to occur, the other cannot
occur.
Thus, the probability of the other event occurring is reduced to 0.
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 7 / 34
Multiplication Law
The multiplication law is based on the definition of conditional
probability, and it provides a way to compute the probability of the
intersection of two events
P(A∩B) = P(A|B)P(B) or P(B∩A) = P(B|A)P(A)
In the case of independent eventsP(A|B) = P(A) or P(B|A) = P(B),
and the multiplication law for independent events becomes
P(A∩B) = P(A)P(B) or P(B∩A) = P(B)P(A)
Therefore, two eventsA and B are dependent when
P(A∩B)̸= P(A)P(B)
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 8 / 34
Multiplication Law
Example: Draw three cardsWITH replacement i.e., draw one card,
look at it, p ut it back, and re p eat twice more.
Problem: Find the probability of drawing 3 Queens in a row
Solution: Let Qi (i = 1,2,3) be the event thati th draw gives you a
Queen. Then the three events are independent of each other.
P(Q1∩Q2∩Q3) = P(Q1)P(Q2)P(Q3) = 4
52×4
52×4
52 = 0.00046

Multiplication Law
Example: Draw three cardsWITHOUT replacement i.e., draw one
card, look at it, keep it, and repeat for 2 more times
Problem: Find the probability of drawing 3 Queens in a row
Solution: Let Qi (i = 1,2,3) be the event thati th draw gives you a
Queen. Then the three events are dependent of each other.
P(Q1∩Q2∩Q3) = P(Q1)P(Q2|Q1)P(Q3|Q1∩Q2) = 4
52×3
51×2
50 = 0.00018

Prior and Posterior Probabilities
Often, we begin probability analysis with initial orprior probabilities.
Then, from a sample, special report, or a product test we obtain some
additional information.
Given this information, we calculate revised orposterior probabilities.
Bayes’ theorem provides the means for revising the prior probabilities.
Figure 2: Bayes’ Process
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 11 / 34
Bayes’ Theorem
Given an event B, and a set of mutually exclusive and exhaustive
events A1, A2, ..., An, and we know the prior probabilities
P(A1), P(A2), ..., P(An). Then the posterior is:
P(A1|B) = P(B|A1)P(A1)
P(B|A1)P(A1) + ... + P(B|An)P(An)
Figure 3: Bayes’ Theorem
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 12 / 34
Bayes’ Theorem: Example
Example: 25 percent of residents in an area leaves their garage doors

percent for those who leave their doors open and 1 percent for those
who leave their doors closed.
Problem: Suppose one resident was robbed, what is the probability
that he or she originally left his or her door open?
Solution: Let B = robbed residents,A1 = open doors residents,A2 =
close door residents. We know that
P(A1) = 0.25, P(A2) = 0.75, P(B|A1) = 0.05, P(B|A2) = 0.01.

What is Hypothesis Testing? And Why Do We
Need It?
We have to make guesses/predictions all the time with very limited
information
Some are right and some are wrong
2 types of wrong answers
Set up theSTATISTICALboundaries for the guess
If your guess is within the boundaries, it’s anacceptable(correct)
guess
If your guess if out of the boundaries, it’s a false/wrong guess
Hypothesis testing is the mathematical representation of those
boundaries
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 4/46
Hypothesis Testing
Hypothesis testing can be used to determine whether a statement
about the value of a population parameter
Should or should not be rejected
Believed to be true or false
The null hypothesis, denoted byH0 , is atentative assumption about
a population parameter
The alternative hypothesis, denoted byHa or H1, is the opposite of
what is stated in the null hypothesis
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 5/46
To-Do’s for Hypothesis Testing
Step 1: grab some sample data from a population
Step 2: use some data from a sample to test the twocompeting
statements
Indicated by H0 and Ha
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 6/46
Developing Null and Alternative Hypotheses
It could be tricky to understand the context of the situation and
structure the hypotheses appropriately so that the test conclusion
provides the desired information.
All hypothesis testing applications involvecollecting a sampleand
using the sample results to provide evidence for drawing a conclusion.
Good questions to consider when formulating the null and alternative
hypotheses are:
What is the purpose of collecting the sample?
What conclusions are we hoping to make?
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 7/46
Developing Null and Alternative Hypotheses -
Example
When an attempt is being made to gather evidence in support of a
hypothesis, it is often best to begin with the alternative hypothesis
and make it the conclusion that the researcher hopes to support.
The conclusion that the research hypothesis is true is made if the
sample data provide sufficient evidence to show that the null
hypothesis can be rejected.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 8/46
An Example
Ford Motors is developing a new fuel injection that is believed to
provide an MPG of more than 24 MPG for their F-150’s.
We set the population mean miles per gallonHa :µ>24 as the
alternative hypothesis
And set the null hypothesis asH0 :µ≤24
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 9/46
Developing Null and Alternative Hypotheses
We might begin with a belief or assumption that a statement about
the value of a population parameter is true.
We then use a hypothesis test to challenge the assumption and
determine if there is statistical evidence to conclude that the
assumption is not true

The label on an Ozarka water bottle states that it contains 16.9 fluid
ounces.
We assume the label correct
We assume the population mean filling weight,µ, is 16.9 fluid ounces,
We set it as the null hypothesis, orH0 :µ= 16.9
Then, we challenge the assumption in the alternative hypothesis
Ha :µ̸= 16.9

Summary of Forms for Null and Alternative
Hypotheses
Rule 1: The equality part of the hypotheses always appears in the null
hypothesis.
Rule 2: A hypothesis test about the value of a population meanµ
must take one of the followingTHREE forms, where # is the
hypothesized value of the population mean.
One-tailed (Lower-tail): H0 :µ≥# vs. Ha :µ<#
One-tailed (Upper-tail): H0 :µ≤# vs. Ha :µ>#
Two-tailed (Both Upper and Lower):H0 :µ= # vs. Ha :µ̸= #
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 12/46
Summary of Forms for Null and Alternative
Hypotheses
One-tailed (Lower-tail): H0 :µ≥# vs. Ha :µ<#
One-tailed (Upper-tail): H0 :µ≤# vs. Ha :µ>#
Two-tailed (Both Upper and Lower):H0 :µ= # vs. Ha :µ̸= #

Hypotheses
In this chapter, we show how statistical inference from sample data
and results can be used together with hypothesis testing to determine
whether a statement about thevalue of a population parameter
should or should not be rejected.
We quantify thefavor or support, if the sample results support or
favorite Ha,
Case1: We rejectH0 -> We supportH1
Case2: We do not rejectH0 -> We cannot supportH1
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 14/46
We Are Humans, We Make Mistakes/Wrong
Guesses.
Because hypothesis tests are based on sample data, we must allow for
the possibility of errors.
There are two types of error that can be made in hypothesis tests:
Type I and Type II errors.
Figure 1: Error Types
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 15/46
Type I and Type II Errors
Figure 2: Error Types
Figure 3: Correct Predictions and
Error Types
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 16/46
Type I and Type II Errors
Type I Error
A Type I error is rejectingH0 when it is true.
The probability of making a Type I error when the null hypothesis is
true as an equality is called the level of significance.
Applications of hypothesis testing that only control for the Type I error
are often called significance tests.
Type II Error
A Type II error is acceptingH0 when it is false.
Statisticians themselves avoid the risk of making a Type II error by
using “do not rejectH0” rather than “acceptH0”. - SNEAKY!!
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 17/46
Example of Type I and Type II Errors
Recall the hypothesis testing example about the new fuel injection
unit. Imagine you’ve developed a new fuel system for cars, and you
believe it makes cars more fuel-efficient than the current standard.
You decide to test if this new system really improves the car’s miles
per gallon (MPG) rating compared to the usual 24 MPG benchmark.
H0 :µ≤24
Ha :µ>24 (1)
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 18/46
Example of Type I and Type II Errors
Type I Error: This is like sounding a false alarm.
It’s when youconclude that your new fuel system does indeed improve
the car’s MPG (makes it better than 24 MPG), but in reality, it doesn’t
— It’s just the same as the old system. So, youmistakenly think
you’ve made an improvement when you haven’t
Type II Error: This is like missing something important.
It’s when you decide that your new fuel system doesn’t make any
difference (so, MPG is 24 or less)
But in fact, it actually does improve the MPG. So, you’ve overlooked a
real improvement, thinking your system didn’t make the car more
fuel-efficient when it really did.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 19/46
Example of Type I and Type II Errors
In summary, a Type I error is thinking you’ve achieved something
special when you haven’t, and a Type II error is failing to recognize an
actual achievement, thinking nothing has changed.
Type I Error: Thinking your new system works (improves MPG) when
it doesn’t.
Type II Error: Not realizing your new system works (actually improves
MPG) when it does.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 20/46
One-Tailed Test About a Population Mean
One-tailed tests about a population mean take one of the following
two forms.
Lower/Left-Tailed Test
H0 :µ≥#
Ha :µ<# (2)
One-tailed tests about a population mean take one of the following
two forms.
Upper/Right-Tailed Test
H0 :µ≤#
Ha :µ># (3)
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 21/46
One-Tailed Test About a Population Mean -
Example
Consider the example of the Federal Trade Commission (FTC) testing
a Hilltop Coffee’s claim that its large can contains at least 3 pounds
of coffee.
The FTC assumes Hilltop Coffee’s claim to be correct (µ≥3 pounds)
and sets the alternative hypothesis to challenge the claim, using the
lower tail test,µ<3 pounds. Thus, we write:
H0 :µ≥3
Ha :µ<3 (4)
The FTC is willing to take a 1% risk of making an error on its testing
of Hilltop Coffee’s claim.

p-Value Approach to a Lower Tail Test About a
Population Mean: σ Known
A p-value is a probability, computed using the test statistic, that
measures the support provided by the sample for the null hypothesis.
Suppose the sample ofn = 36 coffee cans revealed¯x = 2.92 pounds.
Then, the test statistic is
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 25/46
p-Value Approach to a Lower Tail Test About a
Population Mean: σ Known
For a lower tail test, the p-value is the probability thatz is less than
or equal to−2.67.
p−value = P(z≤−2.67) = 0.0038
This p-value indicates a small probability of obtaining a sample mean
of ¯x = 2.92 or smaller when sampling from a population withµ= 3.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 26/46
Rejection Rule Option 1: Using p-Value
In the previous slide, we concluded that the p-value of 0.0038 does
not provide much support for the null hypothesis,but is it small
enough to cause us to rejectH0?
The answerdepends upon the level of significanceαfor the test:
If a p-value is less than or equal to the level of significanceα, the value
of the test statistic is in the rejection region.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 27/46
Rejection Rule Option 1: Using p-Value
Because the FTC stated to be willing to take a 1% risk of making a
Type I error, we set the significance levelα= 0.01.
We write the rejection rule using the p-value as:
Reject H0 if p-value≤α
With the p-value = 0.0038 being less than or equal toα= 0.01, we
reject H0.
Therefore, the FTC finds sufficient statistical evidence to conclude at
the 0.01 level of significance that the filling weight of Hilltop Coffee
cans is less than 3 pounds.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 28/46
Rejection Rule Option 2: Using Critical Value
We can use the standard normal probability distribution to find thez
value with an area ofαin the lower (or upper) tail of the distribution.
The value of the test statistic that establishes the boundary of the
rejection region is called the critical value for the test.
The rejection rule for a one-tailed test using the critical value is:
Lower Tail: RejectH0 if z≤zα
Upper Tail: RejectH0 if z≥zα
zαis such thatP(z≤zα) =α.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 29/46
Rejection Rule Option 2: Using Critical Value
In the Hilltop Coffee case, whenα= 0.01, zα=−2.33.
The rejection rule for a lower tail test becomes
Reject H0 if z≤−2.33, and becausez =−2.67, we rejectH0.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 30/46
Two-Tailed Test About a Population Mean
A two-tailed test about a population mean takes the following form.
H0 :µ=µ0
Ha :µ̸=µ0
(5)
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 31/46
Two-Tailed Test About a Population Mean -
Example
Consider MaxFlight’s need of testing its manufacturing process to
ensure it produces golf balls with a mean driving distance of 295 yards.
If the golf balls drive less than 295 yards, MaxFlight will eventually lose
sales, and if the golf balls’ driving distance exceeds 295 yards, they may
be rejected by the USGA.
Thus, we assume that the mean driving distance is as specified by
MaxFlight, and challenge it in the alternative hypothesis, by writing:
H0 :µ= 295
Ha :µ̸= 295 (6)
The quality control team selectedα= 0.05 as the level of significance
for the test.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 32/46
Two-Tailed Test About a Population Mean -
Example
Suppose a sample ofn = 50 golf balls is collected, and the sample
mean ¯x = 297.6 is computed as an estimate of the population mean
µ.
Data from previous tests conducted when the process was known to
be in adjustment show that the population standard deviation can be
assumed known with a value ofσ= 12 (This isσknown case).
The sampling distribution of¯x as normally distributed, centered
around the hypothesized mean,µ=µ0 = 295, and with standard
error,σ¯x = σ√n = 1.7
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 33/46
Two-Tailed Test About a Population Mean -
Example
We write the test statistic of a hypothesis test about a population
mean: σknown, using the standard normal random variable,z, as:
z = ¯x−µ0
σ¯x
= 297.6−295
1.7 = 1.53
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 34/46
Two-Tailed Test About a Population Mean -
Example
The p-value corresponds to the probability of finding a value for z
that is at least as unlikely asz = 1.53.
For a two-tailed test of a symmetric sampling distribution, values of
z≥1.53 are just as unlikely as values ofz≤−1.53. Thus, we can
write the p-value as:
p-value = p(z≥1.53) + p(z≤−1.53)
Because the normal distribution is symmetric, we can calculate the
p-value as
p-value = p(z≥1.53) + p(z≤−1.53) = 0.0630 +0.0630 = 0.126
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 35/46
Two-Tailed Test About a Population Mean -
Example
Next, we apply the rejection rule:
Because p-value= 0.126> 0.05
(The quality control team selectedα= 0.05 as the level of significance
for the test.)
We cannot rejectH0.
Thus, no action will be taken to adjust the MaxFlight’s manufacturing
process.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 36/46
Rejection Rule Using Critical Value for a Two-Tailed
Test
The rejection rule for a two-tailed test using the critical value is:
Reject H0 if z≤−zα/2 or z≥zα/2
In the golf ball case, we haveα= 0.05.
Thus, α/2 = 0.025, andzα/2 = z0.025 = 1.96
The rejection rule for this two-tail test becomes
Reject H0 if z≤−1.96 or z≥1.96
Because the value of the test statistic for the golf ball study is
z = 1.53, the statistical evidence willNOT permit us to reject the null
hypothesis at the 0.05 level of significance.
Ian Liu Trinity University BAT2301 - Statistics for Business 04/14/2025 37/46

standard deviation, s, as an estimate of the population standard
deviation, σ, the sampling distribution of¯x follows a t distribution
with n−1 degrees of freedom.
The test statistic for hypothesis tests about a population mean when
σis unknown is
t = ¯x−µ0
s/√n

p-Value Approach to a One-Tailed Test About a
Population Mean: σ Unknown
A business travel magazine wants to classify London’s Heathrow
Airport according to the mean rating for the population of business
travelers.
A rating scale with a score from 0 to 10 is used, and if the population
mean rating is greater than 7, Heathrow Airport will be designated as
a superior service airport.
We set the alternative hypothesis toµ>7, so that the rejection of
the null hypothesis leads to the conclusion that Heathrow should be
designated as a superior service airport. The level of significance for
the test is set at 0.05(α)
H0 :µ≤7
Ha :µ>7 (7)

Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 1/26
Events
An event is a collection of sample points.
Simple event: contains only one sample point
Compound event: contains two or more sample points
If we can identify all the sample points of an experiment and assign a
probability to each, we can compute the probability of an event.
1604 Project was an example
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 2/26
Events
Texas DoT was interested in the probability that the project is
completed in 10 months or less.
From the table in the previous slide, we see that there are six sample
points that provide a project completion time of 10 months or less.
Let C denote the event that the project is completed in 10 months or
less. Thus, we have:
C = {(2, 6), (2, 7), (2, 8), (3, 6), (3, 7), (4, 6)}
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 3/26
Events
Some other possible events may be:
L, the event that the project will complete in less than 10 months:
L ={(2,6),(2,7),(3,6)}
M, the event that the project will complete in more than 10 months:
M ={(3,8),(4,7),(4,8)}

Probability of An Event
The probability of an event is equal to the sum of the probabilities of
the sample points in the event.
We can calculate the probability that the 1604 project is completed in
10 months or lessby adding the probabilities of the six sample points
belonging to eventC.
P(C ) = P(2,6) + P(2,7) + P(2,8) + P(3,6) + P(3,7) + P(4,6)
P(C ) = 0.15 +0.15 +0.05 +0.10 +0.20 +0.05 = 0.7
Similarly, the probability that the 1604 project is completed inless
than 10 monthsis
P(L) = P(2,6) + P(2,7) + P(3,6) = 0.15 +0.15 +0.10 = 0.4
And the probability that the 1604 project is completed inmore than
10 monthsis
P(M) = P(3,8) + P(4,7) + P(4,8) = 0.05 +0.10 +0.15 = 0.3
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 6/26
Some Basic Relationships of Probability
In an experiment with a large number of sample points, the
identification of all the sample points, as well as the determination of
their associated probabilities, can become an extremely cumbersome
task.
In this section, we present some basic probability relationships that can
be used to compute the probability of an event without knowledge of
all the sample point probabilities.
The complement of an event
The union of two events
The intersection of two events
The addition law
The multiplication law
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 7/26
Complement of an Event
The complement of eventA is defined as the event consisting of all
sample points that are not inA
The complement ofA is denoted byAC
In any probability application, either eventA or its complementAC
must occur, as shown in the Venn diagram
Figure 1: Venn Diagram
Therefore, we have
P(A) + P(AC ) = 1 or P(A) = 1−P(AC )
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 8/26
Complement of an Event
Example: Roll a die:D1 ={1,3,5}. Then DC
1 ={2,4,6}
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 9/26
Union of Two Events
The union of eventsA and B is the event containing all sample points
that are inA and B or both.
The union of eventsA and B is denoted byA∪B.
Note that the two circles in the Venn diagram overlap, indicating that
some sample points are contained in bothA and B .
Figure 2: Venn Diagram - Union
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 10/26
Union of Two Events
Union: The union event of two eventsA and B is denoted asA∪B,
which consists of all outcomes in eitherA or B or in both. Namely
event A∪B occurs if eitherA or B occurs.
Example: Roll a die:D1 ={1,3,5}and D2 ={1,2,3}. Then
D1∪D2 ={1,2,3,5}
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 11/26
Intersection of Two Events
The intersection of eventsA and B is the event containing all sample
points belonging to bothA and B. The intersection of eventsA and B
is denoted byA∩B.
Note that the area in the Venn diagram where the two circles overlap
is the intersection.
The intersection contains all the sample points that are in bothA and
B.
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 12/26
Intersection of Two Events
The intersection event of two eventsA and B is denoted asA∩B,
which consists of all outcomes in bothA and B or in both.
A∩B occurs if bothA and B occur.
Example: Roll a die:D1 ={1,3,5}and D2 ={1,2,3}, then
D1∩D2 ={1,3}
Two eventsA and B are mutually exclusive ifA∩B =∅.
Figure 3: Venn Diagram - Intersection
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 13/26
Addition Law
The addition law provides a way to compute the probability of the
union of two events:
P(A∪B) = P(A) + P(B)−P(A∩B)
To understand the addition law intuitively, consider the following:
The sample points in the intersectionA∩B are in bothA and B.
When we computeP(A) + P(B)we are in effect counting each of the
sample points inA∩B twice.
We correct for this overcounting by subtractingP(A∩B)
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 14/26
Addition Law: Example
Toss two fair coins in very trial and assume that the four possible
outcomes S ={HH,HT ,TH,TT}are equally likely to happen.
Let A ={HH,HT}be the event that the first coin falls head, and
B ={HH,TH}be the event that the second coin falls head.
Problem: Find the probability that either the first coin falls head or the
second coin falls head?
Solution:
P(A∪B) = P(A) + P(B)−P(A∩B) = 2
4 + 2
4−1
4 = 3
4
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 15/26
An Application of the Addition Law
Consider a group of 50 software engineers who work in online banking.
At the end of an evaluation period, a bank manager found that
5 engineers completed work late (eventL), or P(L) = 5/50 = 0.10
6 engineers produced code that contains errors (eventE), or
P(E ) = 6/50 = 0.12
2 engineers completed work late and produced code that contains errors
(event L∩E), or P(L∩E ) = 2/50 = 0.04
The probability that an engineer completed work late or produced code
that contains errors (eventL∪E) can be calculated using the addition
law as
P(L∪E ) = P(L) + P(E )−P(L∩E ) = 0.10 +0.12−0.04 = 0.18
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 16/26
Conditional Probability
We use the notationP(A|B) to denote the conditional probability of
event A, given that eventB has occurred.
We calculate the probability of eventA given B as
P(A|B) = P(A∩B)
P(B)
Figure 4: Venn Diagram - Conditional Events
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 17/26
Conditional Probability
We calculate the probability of eventA given B as
P(A|B) = P(A∩B)
P(B)
We calculate the probability of eventB given A as
P(B|A) = P(A∩B)
P(A)
Therefore, P(A|B) and P(B|A) are NOT the same thing.
Ian Liu Department of Fin&BA Trinity UniversityBAT2301 - Statistics for Business 18/26
Proof of P(A|B) = P(A∩B)

Joint Probability Table
Dividing the data values in the crosstabulation by the total of 1,200
officers enables us to summarize the available information on
promotions with a joint probability table.
In the body of the table, we have the joint probabilities, which are the
intersection probabilities of the events of whether a police officer was
promoted A or not AC with whether the police officer is maleM or
female F.
The joint probability that an officer was promotedA and is a maleM
P(A∩M) = 288/1200 = 0.24
